Key Takeaway
Of the three types of non-verbal indication, only verbal conventional indication is the proper subject of logic due to its consistency and general applicability.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Definition of Implicative Signification and the Condition of Mental Concomitance
Commentator defines implicative signification and its condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of implicative signification and explain why the concomitance must be mental rather than external.

Core Idea
Implicative signification depends on mental concomitance, not external causation.

This lesson unpacks the definition of the third type of signification in al-mantiq: implicative signification. The commentator explains that a word signifies a meaning concomitant to its primary meaning, but only when the mind naturally connects them without external evidence. This condition is crucial for distinguishing valid implicative signification from accidental association.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall that signification is divided into mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implicative). Focus on the third as defined in the passage.
2
Locate the definition in the Arabic text
Find the phrase: 'وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لَازِم لِلْمَعْنَى الَّذِي وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ'. This is the core definition.
3
Extract the condition of mental concomitance
The commentator adds: 'بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيَّا'. This means that whenever the primary meaning is understood, the concomitant is understood without external evidence.
Mental concomitance is distinguished from logical or external concomitance.
4
Contrast mental with external concomitance
External concomitance (e.g., smoke from fire) does not hold by the word itself; the mind may not connect them without experience. Mental concomitance is intrinsic to the word's usage.
5
Test with a fiqh example
Consider 'khamr' (wine): the primary meaning is an intoxicating drink; the prohibition (hurmah) is a mental concomitant because the mind connects them via revelation, not external observation.

وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الثَّالِثِ بِقَوْلِهِ : (وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ : ((الْتِزَامُ) أَيْ : وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لَازِم لِلْمَعْنَى الَّذِي وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ؛ أَيْ: مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ هِيَ التِزَامٌ ; أَيْ: دَلَالَةٌ الْتِزَامِ بِشَرْطِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ ذِهْنِيَّا، كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ قَصَارَةٌ . لَا اسْتِلْزَامَ عَلَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَكُونَ الْمَعْنَى هَكَذَا : كُلَّمَا أُطْلِقَ اللَّفْظُ فُهِمَ مَعْنَاهُ إِجْمَالاً، وَكُلَّمَا فُهِمَ مَعْنَاهُ إِجْمَالاً فُهِمَ جُزْؤُهُ تَفْصِيلاً، وَلَا خَفَاءَ فِي كَذِبِ الْكُبْرَى حِينَئِذٍ ; إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ إِجْمَالاً فَهْمُ الْجُزْءِ تَفْصِيلاً; لِأَنَّ النَّفْسَ قَدْ تَلْتَفِتُ إِلَى تَفْصِيلِ الْجُزْءِ بَعْدَ فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ، وَقَدْ لَا تَلْتَفِتُ، وَمَا أُجِيبَ بِهِ عَنْ هَذَا الْإِيرَادِ لَا يَنْهَضُ. الْقَوْلُ الثَّانِي : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ التَّضَمُّنِ لَا انْتِقَالَ فِيهَا أَصْلاً، وَلَيْسَ لِلْجُزْءِ فَهْمٌ يَخُصُّهُ، وَإِنَّمَا هُنَاكَ فَهْمٌ وَاحِدٌ، إِنْ قُيسَ إِلَى الْمَجْمُوعِ كَانَ مُطَابَقَةً، وَإِنْ قِيسَ إِلَى أَحَادِ الْأَجْزَاءِ كَانَ تَضَمُّناً، فَالدَّلاَلَةُ عَلَى الْكُلِّ لَا تُغَايِرُ الدَّلاَلَةَ عَلَى الْجُزْءِ مُغَايَرَةً بِالذَّاتِ، بَلْ بِالْإِضَافَةِ وَالِاعْتِبَارِ، فَهِيَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ الْمَعْنَى مُطَابَقَةً، وَبِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ تَضَمُّناً، فَدَلَالةُ التَّضَمُّنِ عَلَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ: فَهْمُ الْجُزْءِ فِي ضِمْنِ الْكُلِّ، وَحِينَئِذٍ تَحَقَّقَ اسْتِلْزَامُ الْمُطَابَقَةِ لِلتَّضَمُّنِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ: الْآمِدِيُّ وَابْنُ الْحَاجِبِ وَالْعَضُدُ، وَالسَّعْدُ فِي «حَاشِيَتِهِ»، وَالسَّيِّدُ فِي «حَاشِيَةِ الْمُطَوَّلِ»، وَابْنُ أَبِي شَرِيفٍ، وَغَيْرُهُمْ، وَصَوَّبَهُ الشَّارِحُ هُنَا، وَدَرَجَ عَلَيْهِ شَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي حَمْدُونٌ فِي «خَرِيدَتِهِ» وَنَصُّهُ : وَحُزْنُهُ تَضَمُّناً وَلَا انْتِقَالَ . الْقَوْلُ الثَّالِثُ : يَقُولُ بِتَعَدُّدِ الْفَهْمِ أَيْضاً كَالْأَوَّلِ ; إِلَّا أَنَّ فَهْمَ الْجُزْءِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ سَابِقٌ عَلَى فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ مِنْهُ، فَيَكُونُ الِانْتِقَالُ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى الْجُزْءِ، وَمِنَ الْجُزْءِ إِلَى الْكُلِّ، عَكْسُ الْقَوْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَهَذَا الْقَوْلُ بَاطِلٌ ; إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اللَّفْظِ فَهْمُ جُزْءِ الْمَعْنَى ; لِعَدَمِ وَضْعِهِ لَهُ، وَلَا مِنْ فَهْمِ الْجُزْءِ فَهْمُ الْكُلِّ ; لِأَنَّ الْجُزْءَ أَعَمُّ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَهْمِ الْأَعَمِّ فَهْمُ الْأَخَصِّ، وَلِبُطْلَانِهِ لَمْ يَتَعَرَّضْ لَهُ الشَّارِحُ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى أَصْلاً. قوله : (وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لازم ... إلخ) هذا التَّقريرُ أنسبُ بكلام النَّاظم ممَّا قرَّره به أبو مدين ونصه : وإن دل على ما لزم فهو - أي : اللفظ ; أي دلالته - التزام؛ أي: دلالة التزام، فيكون الكلام على حذف شرط ومضافين .
And he pointed to the third with his saying: 'And whatever is concomitant, then it is implicative.' That is: the signification of a word upon a meaning concomitant to the meaning for which it was coined, external to it; that is, insofar as it is concomitant to it, it is implicative; that is, implicative signification, on condition that the concomitance be mental, as indicated by Qasara? There is no necessary entailment in this statement, because the meaning would be: whenever the word is uttered, its meaning is understood in summary, and whenever its meaning is understood in summary, its part is understood in detail. And there is no concealment of the falsity of the major premise then, since understanding the whole in summary does not necessitate understanding the part in detail; because the soul may attend to the detail of the part after understanding the whole, or it may not attend. And what is answered regarding this objection does not suffice. The second opinion: that the signification of inclusion has no transition at all, and the part has no understanding specific to it; rather, there is a single understanding: if it is measured against the whole, it is correspondence, and if it is measured against the individual parts, it is inclusion. So the signification upon the whole does not differ from the signification upon the part essentially, but rather by relation and consideration. So relative to the whole meaning it is correspondence, and relative to its part it is inclusion. Thus the signification of inclusion according to this opinion is the understanding of the part within the whole. And in that case, the necessary entailment of correspondence for inclusion is realized. And upon this opinion are al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-'Adud, al-Sa'd in his gloss, al-Sayyid in his gloss on al-Mutawwal, Ibn Abi Sharif, and others; and the commentator approved it here, and our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun followed it in his Khurida, and his text: 'And its sorrow is inclusion, and no transition.' The third opinion: it says multiplicity of understanding also like the first, except that understanding the part from the word precedes understanding the whole from it; so the transition is from the word to the part, and from the part to the whole, the opposite of the first opinion. And this opinion is false, for from the utterance of the word the understanding of a part of the meaning does not follow, because there is no coinage for it, nor from understanding the part does understanding the whole follow, because the part is more general, and understanding the more general does not necessitate understanding the more specific. And due to its falsity, the commentator did not address it at all. His statement: 'And the signification of the word upon a concomitant meaning, etc.' This formulation is more appropriate to the words of the versifier than what Abu Madin formulated, and his text: 'And if it signifies what is concomitant, then it — that is, the word, i.e., its signification — is implicative; i.e., implicative signification; so the statement involves ellipsis of a condition and two genitive constructions.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya) and a discussion of the psychological process involved. The commentator begins by explaining the third type of signification mentioned in the matn: a word signifies a meaning that is concomitant (lazim) to its primary meaning, external to it. The key condition is that the concomitance must be mental (dhihni), meaning that whenever the word is uttered and its primary meaning understood, the concomitant meaning is also understood in the mind without need for external evidence. He then presents three opinions on how this understanding occurs. The first opinion holds that there is a mental transition (intiqal) from the word to the primary meaning, then to the part or concomitant. The commentator criticizes this by showing that understanding the whole in summary does not necessarily entail understanding the part in detail, as the mind may not attend to the detail. The second opinion, attributed to major scholars like al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib, denies any transition; rather, the same single understanding is considered correspondence (mutabaqa) when related to the whole and inclusion (tadammun) when related to the part. On this view, the signification of inclusion is simply understanding the part within the whole, and correspondence necessarily entails inclusion. The commentator endorses this opinion. The third opinion inverts the first, claiming understanding of the part comes before the whole, but is dismissed as false because the word is not coined for the part, and understanding the part (more general) does not necessitate understanding the whole (more specific). Finally, the commentator offers an alternative interpretation of the matn's wording, suggesting it implies ellipsis.

Key Terms
الدلالة الالتزامية
اللزوم الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the condition for implicative signification?
A) The concomitance must be physical
B) The concomitance must be logical
C) The concomitance must be mental
D) The concomitance must be linguistic

Key Takeaway
Implicative signification requires a mental connection between the primary meaning and the concomitant, not mere external association.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from Islamic law (other than wine) where implicative signification is used to derive a ruling, and explain why the concomitance is mental rather than external.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
12 min
Three Opinions on the Psychological Process of Implicative Signification
Commentator presents and evaluates three opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three opinions on how the part or concomitant is understood and identify which the commentator endorses.

Core Idea
The debate is whether understanding a part involves a mental transition or is simply the same understanding considered differently.

After defining implicative signification, the commentator presents three opinions about the cognitive process: whether there is a mental transition (intiqal) from the word to the whole and then to the part, one single understanding without transition, or a reverse transition. Understanding this debate clarifies how logical tools operate in the mind and how definitions are processed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
The first opinion claims there is a mental transition from the word to the whole, then to the part. The commentator refutes it by showing that understanding the whole in summary does not necessitate understanding the part in detail.
The major premise 'whenever the whole is understood in summary, the part is understood in detail' is false.
2
Identify the second opinion
The second opinion denies any transition. There is a single understanding that is considered mutabaqa when related to the whole and tadammun when related to the part. The commentator lists scholars who hold this view (al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, etc.) and explicitly approves it.
This opinion avoids the logical flaw of the first.
3
Identify the third opinion
The third opinion claims the part is understood before the whole, reversing the direction. The commentator dismisses it as false because the word is not coined for the part, and understanding the part (more general) does not necessitate understanding the whole (more specific).
This opinion is so weak the commentator did not even address it in the matn.
4
Compare the three opinions
Note the differences: first = transition from whole to part; second = no transition; third = transition from part to whole. The second is preferred.
5
Determine why the second opinion is preferred
It avoids the problematic entailment of the first (the necessary transition does not hold) and the logical error of the third (part is more general). Moreover, it aligns with the idea that the signification of inclusion is simply the same understanding under a different description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ إِجْمَالاً فَهْمُ الْجُزْءِ تَفْصِيلاً; لِأَنَّ النَّفْسَ قَدْ تَلْتَفِتُ إِلَى تَفْصِيلِ الْجُزْءِ بَعْدَ فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ، وَقَدْ لَا تَلْتَفِتُ، وَمَا أُجِيبَ بِهِ عَنْ هَذَا الْإِيرَادِ لَا يَنْهَضُ. الْقَوْلُ الثَّانِي : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ التَّضَمُّنِ لَا انْتِقَالَ فِيهَا أَصْلاً، وَلَيْسَ لِلْجُزْءِ فَهْمٌ يَخُصُّهُ، وَإِنَّمَا هُنَاكَ فَهْمٌ وَاحِدٌ، إِنْ قُيسَ إِلَى الْمَجْمُوعِ كَانَ مُطَابَقَةً، وَإِنْ قِيسَ إِلَى أَحَادِ الْأَجْزَاءِ كَانَ تَضَمُّناً، فَالدَّلاَلَةُ عَلَى الْكُلِّ لَا تُغَايِرُ الدَّلاَلَةَ عَلَى الْجُزْءِ مُغَايَرَةً بِالذَّاتِ، بَلْ بِالْإِضَافَةِ وَالِاعْتِبَارِ، فَهِيَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ الْمَعْنَى مُطَابَقَةً، وَبِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ تَضَمُّناً، فَدَلَالةُ التَّضَمُّنِ عَلَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ: فَهْمُ الْجُزْءِ فِي ضِمْنِ الْكُلِّ، وَحِينَئِذٍ تَحَقَّقَ اسْتِلْزَامُ الْمُطَابَقَةِ لِلتَّضَمُّنِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ: الْآمِدِيُّ وَابْنُ الْحَاجِبِ وَالْعَضُدُ، وَالسَّعْدُ فِي «حَاشِيَتِهِ»، وَالسَّيِّدُ فِي «حَاشِيَةِ الْمُطَوَّلِ»، وَابْنُ أَبِي شَرِيفٍ، وَغَيْرُهُمْ، وَصَوَّبَهُ الشَّارِحُ هُنَا، وَدَرَجَ عَلَيْهِ شَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي حَمْدُونٌ فِي «خَرِيدَتِهِ» وَنَصُّهُ : وَحُزْنُهُ تَضَمُّناً وَلَا انْتِقَالَ . الْقَوْلُ الثَّالِثُ : يَقُولُ بِتَعَدُّدِ الْفَهْمِ أَيْضاً كَالْأَوَّلِ ; إِلَّا أَنَّ فَهْمَ الْجُزْءِ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ سَابِقٌ عَلَى فَهْمِ الْكُلِّ مِنْهُ، فَيَكُونُ الِانْتِقَالُ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ إِلَى الْجُزْءِ، وَمِنَ الْجُزْءِ إِلَى الْكُلِّ، عَكْسُ الْقَوْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَهَذَا الْقَوْلُ بَاطِلٌ ; إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِطْلَاقِ اللَّفْظِ فَهْمُ جُزْءِ الْمَعْنَى ; لِعَدَمِ وَضْعِهِ لَهُ، وَلَا مِنْ فَهْمِ الْجُزْءِ فَهْمُ الْكُلِّ ; لِأَنَّ الْجُزْءَ أَعَمُّ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَهْمِ الْأَعَمِّ فَهْمُ الْأَخَصِّ، وَلِبُطْلَانِهِ لَمْ يَتَعَرَّضْ لَهُ الشَّارِحُ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى أَصْلاً. قوله : (وَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنَى لازم ... إلخ) هذا التَّقريرُ أنسبُ بكلام النَّاظم ممَّا قرَّره به أبو مدين ونصه : وإن دل على ما لزم فهو - أي : اللفظ ; أي دلالته - التزام؛ أي: دلالة التزام، فيكون الكلام على حذف شرط ومضافين .
And he pointed to the third with his saying: 'And whatever is concomitant, then it is implicative.' That is: the signification of a word upon a meaning concomitant to the meaning for which it was coined, external to it; that is, insofar as it is concomitant to it, it is implicative; that is, implicative signification, on condition that the concomitance be mental, as indicated by Qasara? There is no necessary entailment in this statement, because the meaning would be: whenever the word is uttered, its meaning is understood in summary, and whenever its meaning is understood in summary, its part is understood in detail. And there is no concealment of the falsity of the major premise then, since understanding the whole in summary does not necessitate understanding the part in detail; because the soul may attend to the detail of the part after understanding the whole, or it may not attend. And what is answered regarding this objection does not suffice. The second opinion: that the signification of inclusion has no transition at all, and the part has no understanding specific to it; rather, there is a single understanding: if it is measured against the whole, it is correspondence, and if it is measured against the individual parts, it is inclusion. So the signification upon the whole does not differ from the signification upon the part essentially, but rather by relation and consideration. So relative to the whole meaning it is correspondence, and relative to its part it is inclusion. Thus the signification of inclusion according to this opinion is the understanding of the part within the whole. And in that case, the necessary entailment of correspondence for inclusion is realized. And upon this opinion are al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-'Adud, al-Sa'd in his gloss, al-Sayyid in his gloss on al-Mutawwal, Ibn Abi Sharif, and others; and the commentator approved it here, and our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun followed it in his Khurida, and his text: 'And its sorrow is inclusion, and no transition.' The third opinion: it says multiplicity of understanding also like the first, except that understanding the part from the word precedes understanding the whole from it; so the transition is from the word to the part, and from the part to the whole, the opposite of the first opinion. And this opinion is false, for from the utterance of the word the understanding of a part of the meaning does not follow, because there is no coinage for it, nor from understanding the part does understanding the whole follow, because the part is more general, and understanding the more general does not necessitate understanding the more specific. And due to its falsity, the commentator did not address it at all. His statement: 'And the signification of the word upon a concomitant meaning, etc.' This formulation is more appropriate to the words of the versifier than what Abu Madin formulated, and his text: 'And if it signifies what is concomitant, then it — that is, the word, i.e., its signification — is implicative; i.e., implicative signification; so the statement involves ellipsis of a condition and two genitive constructions.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya) and a discussion of the psychological process involved. The commentator begins by explaining the third type of signification mentioned in the matn: a word signifies a meaning that is concomitant (lazim) to its primary meaning, external to it. The key condition is that the concomitance must be mental (dhihni), meaning that whenever the word is uttered and its primary meaning understood, the concomitant meaning is also understood in the mind without need for external evidence. He then presents three opinions on how this understanding occurs. The first opinion holds that there is a mental transition (intiqal) from the word to the primary meaning, then to the part or concomitant. The commentator criticizes this by showing that understanding the whole in summary does not necessarily entail understanding the part in detail, as the mind may not attend to the detail. The second opinion, attributed to major scholars like al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib, denies any transition; rather, the same single understanding is considered correspondence (mutabaqa) when related to the whole and inclusion (tadammun) when related to the part. On this view, the signification of inclusion is simply understanding the part within the whole, and correspondence necessarily entails inclusion. The commentator endorses this opinion. The third opinion inverts the first, claiming understanding of the part comes before the whole, but is dismissed as false because the word is not coined for the part, and understanding the part (more general) does not necessitate understanding the whole (more specific). Finally, the commentator offers an alternative interpretation of the matn's wording, suggesting it implies ellipsis.

Key Terms
دلالة التضمن
اللزوم الذهني
الانتفال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which opinion does the commentator approve in this passage?
A) The first opinion (transition from whole to part)
B) The second opinion (no transition)
C) The third opinion (transition from part to whole)
D) He does not express preference

Key Takeaway
The preferred view is that understanding a part is not a separate mental act from understanding the whole; they are the same understanding considered relatively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'ghasb' (usurpation) as 'illegal taking of property'. According to the second opinion, explain how understanding the whole definition already includes understanding its components without transition.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Condition of Mental Concomitance and Division of Necessary Concomitance
Commentator explains the condition of mental concomitance and divides necessary concomitance into bayyin and ghayr bayyin, with further subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mental concomitance from evident and non-evident concomitance, and classify concomitants into mental, evident non-mental, and non-evident based on the conditions in this passage.

Core Idea
Mental concomitance requires understanding the primary meaning to immediately bring the concomitant to mind.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani's matn clarifies a key condition for implicative signification: the concomitant must be mental. The commentator then unpacks the relationship between mental, evident, and non-evident concomitants, presenting two scholarly positions. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating definitions in the Islamic sciences, where the type of necessary relation determines whether a definition is complete or requires external evidence.

Guided Path
1
Define mental concomitance
From the passage, extract the definition: mental concomitant is one where, whenever the primary meaning is understood from the word, its concomitant is understood mentally. Note the example: evenness for four.
This definition sets the condition for implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam).
2
Distinguish 'rational' from 'mental'
The commentator explains that the versifier used 'rational' (aqli) but intended 'mental' (dhihni) because 'rational' is broader. Recognize that the custom among logicians is to consider only the mental concomitant for definition.
This prevents confusion between the two terms in later texts.
3
Study the two opinions on synonymy
The passage presents two schools: one equates mental with evident; the majority holds that evident is more general. Understand that the evident is defined as needing knowledge of both subject and concomitant to yield the relation, while non-evident requires an external indicator.
The commentator does not take sides but notes that al-Sanusi followed the first opinion, while others preferred the second.
4
Grasp the subdivision of evident
Evident is divided into mental (knowledge of subject alone suffices) and non-mental (needs knowledge of both). Examples: evenness is mental; difference between human and horse is evident but non-mental.
This shows that not all evident concomitants are mental; the condition for mental is that the subject alone triggers the concomitant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِقَوْلِهِ : إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمْ أَيْ : إِنِ الْتَزَمَ بِذِهْنٍ. وَمُرَادُهُمْ بِـ «اللُّزُومِ الذِّهْنِيِّ : أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَعْنَى كُلَّمَا فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ فُهِمَ ذِهْناً لَازِمُهُ؛ لَزِمَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالْخَارِج مَعاً كَـ: «الزَّوْجِيَّةِ الْمَدْلُولِ عَلَيْهَا بِلَفْظِ «الْأَرْبَعَةِ»، فَإِنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لِمَعْنَى الْأَرْبَعَةِ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَفِي الْخَارِجِ عَنْهُ. قوله : (أَيْ : إِنِ الْعَزَمَ بِذِهْنِ ظاهرُهُ : مُرَادَفَةُ الْعَقْلِيِّ لِلذِّهْنِيِّ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، بَلِ الْعَقْلِيُّ أَعَمُّ كَمَا يَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ، فَالنَّاظِمُ أَطْلَقَ الْعَامَّ وَأَرَادَ بِهِ الْخَاصَّ وَالْقَرِينَةُ الْعُرْفُ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ عِنْدَ الْمَنَاطِقَةِ هُوَ اللَّازِمُ الذِّهْنِيُّ. فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : هَلِ اللَّازِمُ الذِّهْنِيُّ يُرَادِفُ اللَّازِمَ الْبَيِّنَ أَمْ لَا؟ قُلْتُ : فِي ذَلِكَ مَذْهَبَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا وَهُوَ مَذْهَبُ كَثِيرٍ مِنَ الْمَنَاطِقَةِ : تُرَادِفُهُمَا، وَعَلَيْهِ دَرَجَ السَّنُوسِيُّ، وَشَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي الطيِّبُ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ كَلَامِ الشَّارِحِ هُنَا. ثَانِيهِمَا وَهُوَ مَذْهَبُ الْأَكْثَرِ : أَنَّ الْبَيِّنَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الذِّهْنِيِّ. فَالْبَيِّنُ هُوَ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ بِاللَّازِمِ وَالْمَلْزُومِ مَعاً الْعِلْمُ بِهِ. وَغَيْرُ الْبَيِّنِ مَا لَا يَكْفِي فِيهِ ذَلِكَ، بَلْ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى قَرِينَةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ كَلُزُومِ الْحُدُوثِ لِلْأَجْرَامِ ; لِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ أَمْرٍ ثَالِثٍ وَهُوَ التَّغَيُّرُ. ثُمَّ الْبَيِّنُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : ذِهْنِيٍّ وَغَيْرِهِ فَإِنْ كَفَى فِي الْعِلْمِ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ بِالْمَلْزُومِ ; بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ كُلَّمَا حَصَلَ الْعِلْمُ بِالْمَلْزُومِ حَصَلَ الْعِلْمُ بِاللَّازِمِ، وَبِلُزُومِهِ لِلْمَلْزُومِ ، فَهُوَ ذِهْنِيٌّ كَلُزُومِ الزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ، وَالْبَصَرِ لِلْعَمَى، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكْفِ فِيهِ الْعِلْمُ بِالْمَلْزُومِ وَحْدَهُ، بَلِ افْتَقَرَ إِلَى الْعِلْمِ بِاللَّازِمِ أَيْضاً كَمَغَايَرَةِ الْإِنْسَانِ لِلْفَرَسِ، فَإِنَّ مَغَايَرَةَ الْإِنْسَانِ لِلْفَرَسِ أَمْرٌ لَازِمٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ، لَكِنْ مَنْ تَصَوَّرَ «الْإِنْسَانَ» لَا يَلْزَمُهُ بِمُجَرَّدِ ذَلِكَ أَنْ يَخْطِرَ بِبَالِهِ مُغَايَرَتُهُ لِلْفَرَسِ، بَلْ يَتَصَوَّرُ «الْإِنْسَانَ» وَهُوَ غَافِلٌ عَنِ «الْفَرَسِ» جُمْلَةً، فَكَيْفَ عَنْ مُغَايَرَتِهِ إِيَّاهُ، فَهُوَ بَيِّنٌ غَيْرُ ذِهْنِيٍّ، وَهَذِهِ الطَّرِيقَةُ أَتَمُّ فَائِدَةً وَعَلَى الطَّرِيقَةِ الْأُولَى دَرَجَ صَاحِبُ «الْقَادِرِيَّةِ» تَبَعاً لِأَصْلِهِ، فَقَالَ: وَاللُّزُومُ يَنْقَسِمُ :
By his saying: 'If with reason it is implicated,' i.e., if it is implicated mentally. And their intention by 'mental concomitance' is that the meaning be such that whenever it is understood from the word, its concomitant is understood mentally; it is concomitant in the mind and in the external together, like 'evenness' signified by the word 'four', for it is concomitant to the meaning of four in the mind and in the external. His statement: 'i.e., if it is implicated with reason' — its apparent meaning is synonymy of 'rational' and 'mental', but it is not so; rather, 'rational' is more general, as will be explained. So the versifier used the general and intended the specific, and the contextual indicator is custom, for what is considered by the logicians is the mental concomitant. If you say: Is the mental concomitant synonymous with the evident concomitant or not? I say: There are two schools: one of them, the school of many logicians, is that they are synonymous, and al-Sanusi and our teacher Sayyidi al-Tayyib followed it, and it is apparent from the commentator's speech here. The second, the school of the majority, is that the evident is more general than the mental. So the evident is that from knowledge of the concomitant and the subject together, knowledge of it follows. And the non-evident is that where that is insufficient, but rather it requires a separate contextual indicator, like the concomitance of temporality to bodies, because a third thing is necessary, namely change. Then the evident divides into mental and other: if knowledge of the subject alone suffices for knowledge of it, meaning that whenever knowledge of the subject is obtained, knowledge of the concomitant and of its concomitance to the subject is obtained, then it is mental, like the concomitance of evenness to four, and of sight to blindness. And if knowledge of the subject alone is not sufficient, but rather it needs knowledge of the concomitant as well, like the difference between human and horse, for the difference between human and horse is a concomitant of human, but someone who conceives 'human' is not necessarily, by that alone, that his mind should occur to his difference from horse; rather he conceives 'human' while being entirely heedless of 'horse', so how about his difference from it? So it is evident but not mental. And this method is more complete in benefit, and upon the first method followed the author of al-Qadiriyya, following his source, so he said: And the concomitant divides: ...
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage clarifies the condition of mental concomitance (luzum dhihni) required for implicative signification and then provides a detailed classification of necessary concomitance. The commentator begins by explaining the matn's phrase 'if with reason it is implicated' means mental implication (iltizam bi-dhihn). Mental concomitance is defined as: whenever the primary meaning is understood from the word, its concomitant is also understood mentally. This includes cases where the concomitance holds both in mind and in reality, such as the evenness of four: understanding four brings the idea of evenness immediately. He then notes that the term 'rational' (aqli) is broader than 'mental' (dhihni), but in context it is used for mental due to customary usage. Next, he addresses a question: Is the mental concomitant synonymous with the evident (bayyin) concomitant? He presents two positions: one group equates them; the majority holds that evident is more general. Evident is defined as that wherein knowledge of the subject and the concomitant together yields knowledge of the relation, while non-evident requires an external indicator (e.g., temporality of bodies needs observation of change). Evident is then divided into mental and non-mental. A mental evident is when knowledge of the subject alone suffices to know the concomitant and its relation; examples include evenness for four and sight for blindness. A non-mental evident is when knowledge of the subject alone does not suffice; e.g., the difference between human and horse: one can conceive human without thinking of horse or the difference; it is evident once both concepts are present, but not mental. The commentator endorses this second classification as more beneficial.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذهني
اللازم البين
اللازم غير البين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which example does the commentator give of an evident but non-mental concomitant?
A) Evenness of four
B) Sight with blindness
C) Difference between human and horse
D) Temporality of bodies

Key Takeaway
Mental concomitance requires that the primary meaning alone suffices to bring the concomitant to mind; evident concomitance is broader and includes cases where both subject and concomitant must be known to perceive the relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal'. Identify whether the concomitant 'rationality' is a mental concomitant or evident non-mental according to the classification in this passage. Explain your reasoning using the conditions given.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'wajib' often includes the concomitant 'punishment for leaving'. Determine whether this concomitant is mental or evident non-mental. Provide an example of a mental concomitant from fiqh (e.g., the evenness of four suggests something about numbers; what about the obligation of prayer and its constituent parts?).

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Mental vs. External Concomitance in Conventional Indication
Author defines the three types of conventional indication and explains the logical condition for necessary concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and external concomitance and determine whether a given example qualifies as دلالة التزام in logic.

Core Idea
Logic only accepts mental concomitance for دلالة التزام; external concomitance belongs to uṣūl and bayān.

In this passage, al-Bannānī clarifies a crucial distinction: for a word to indicate a meaning by necessary concomitance (دلالة التزام) in logic, the concomitance must be mental — the mind must immediately grasp the connection. External concomitance, where the connection exists in reality but not necessarily in the mind, is not considered logical indication. This distinction has practical implications for how we derive meanings from texts and how we construct arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conventional indication
From the passage, note that conventional indication (دلالة وضعية) includes: indication by مطابقة (full correspondence), by تضمن (inclusion), and by التزام (concomitance). The focus here is on the third type.
The passage does not list the first two in detail but refers to them in the discussion.
2
Extract the definition of mental concomitance
Find where the author explains mental concomitance (لزوم ذهني) using the example of blindness and sight. Note that whenever blindness is conceived, sight is necessarily conceived because blindness is absence of sight.
The author clarifies that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness (that would be تضمن), but a necessary concomitant.
3
Distinguish external concomitance
Look at the examples: crow/blackness, snow/whiteness, change/temporal origination, trace/effecter. For the first two, the mind can imagine a white crow or blue snow, so the connection is not mental. For the last two, one can think of change without thinking of temporal origination if ignorant or heedless.
These are external concomitances: real but not mentally necessary.
4
Understand the disciplinary difference
Note the author's statement: In logic, only mental concomitance counts as دلالة التزام, while in uṣūl and bayān, external concomitance is accepted. This explains why metaphor and metonymy are valid in those fields.
The super-commentator adds that if mental concomitance were required, many derived meanings from the Quran and Sunnah would be excluded.
5
Analyze the refinement of the change example
Al-Bannānī critiques al-Hilālī's view that change does not mentally necessitate temporal origination. He argues that for a trained mind, it is self-evident; the lack of concomitance is relative to persons and states.
This shows how the condition of mental concomitance can be context-dependent.

أنواع الدلالة الوضعية
وَيُقَالُ لِهَذَا اللَّازِمِ : «اللَّازِمُ المُطْلَقُ».
- أَوْ لَزِمَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَط دُونَ الخَارِج كَـ: «البَصَرِ» المَدْلُولِ عَلَيْهِ بِلَفْظِ العَمَى»، فَإِنَّهُ كُلَّمَا تُصُوِّرَ العَمَى فِي الذِّهْنِ تُصُوِّرَ مَعَهُ البَصَرُ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لِلْعَمَى إِلَّا عَدَمُ البَصَرِ عَمَّا هُوَ مِنْ شَأْنِهِ، فَالبَصَرُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ ذِهْناً ، وَهُوَ مُنَافٍ لَهُ خَارِجاً ، وَلَيْسَ البَصَرُ مَدْلُولاً عَلَيْهِ بِالعَمَى تَضَمُّناً ؛ إذْ لَيْسَ العَمَى هُوَ العَدَمُ وَالبَصَرُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ البَصَرُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَعْنَاهُ، وَإِنَّمَا مَعْنَاهُ العَدَمُ المُضَافُ إِلَى البَصَرِ ، وَالمُضَافُ إِلَيْهِ خَارِجٌ عَنِ المُضَافِ. وَأَمَّا دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَعْنًى خَارِجٍ عَنْ مَعْنَاهُ لَازِمٌ لَهُ فِي الخَارِجِ دُونَ الذِّهْنِ فَلَا تُسَمَّى فِي المَنْطِقِ : دَلَالَةَ الْتِزَامِ، وَتُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ فِي عِلْمَيِ الأُصُولِ وَالبَيَانِ، وَذَلِكَ كَدَلَالَةِ لَفْظِ «الغُرَابِ عَلَى السَّوَادِ»، و«التَّلْجِ» عَلَى «البَيَاضِ»، وَ: «التَّغَيُّرِ» عَلَى «الحُدُوثِ»، وَ: «الأَثَرِ» عَلَى وُجُودِ المُؤَثِّرِ». أَمَّا الأَوَّلَانِ : فَلِأَنَّ العَقْلَ يُجَوِّزُ كَوْنَ الغُرَابِ أَبْيَضَ وَالتَّلْجِ أَزْرَقَ مَثَلاً ، وَإِذَا قَطَعْتَ النَّظَرَ عَمَّا فِي الخَارِجِ وَرَجِعْتَ إِلَى نَفْسِ حَقِيقَتِهِمَا ، عَلِمْتَ انْتِفَاءَ اللُّزُوم . وَأَمَّا الأَخِيرَانِ : فَلِأَنَّ العَقْلَ لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا تَصَوَّرَ التَّغَيُّرَ وَالأَثَرَ عَلِمَ الحُدُوثَ وَالمُؤَثْرَ، بَلْ يَتَصَوُّرُ الأَوَّلَيْنِ ذَاهِلاً عَنِ الأَخِيرَيْنِ ، أَوْ جَاهِلاً بِلُزُومِهِمَا لَهُمَا، وَهَذَا مَعْنَى عَدَمِ اللُّزُومِ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ العَقَلُ بَعْدَ الِاسْتِدْلَالِ يَقْطَعُ بِاللُّزُومِ فِي الخَارِجِ، وَلَا يُجَوِّزُ انْتِفَاءَهُ.
قوله : ((اللَّازِمُ المُطْلَقُ» سُمِّيَ : «مطلقاً؛ لعدم تقييد لزومه بالذهن ولا بالخارج. قوله : (تُصُوِّرَ مَعَهُ البَصَرُ) أي: مفهومه، وهو : القوة الباصرة التي من شأنها إدراك ما ينطبع فيه من أصول أشباح الأجسام.
قوله : (وَلَيْسَ البَصَرُ أصل هذا للقطب في «شرح الشَّمسيَّة» ونصه :
فإن قلت : البصر جزء مفهوم العمى، فلا تكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام، بل بالتّضمن.
قلنا : العمى عدم البصر، لا العدم والبصر.
قوله : (العَدَمُ المُضَافُ إِلَى البَصَرِ ) أي : المضاف دون المضاف إليه، ولكن لا بدَّ من اعتبار الإضافة، وإلا لكان مفهومه العدم مطلقاً، وهو باطل، فالإضافة معتبرة دون المضاف إليه . قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ فِي عِلْمَي الأُصُولِ وَالبَيَانِ) ولذلك كثرت الفوائد التي يستنبطونها مِنَ الكتاب والسُّنَّة وألفاظ الأئمة، ولو اشترطوا اللُّزوم الذهني لخرج كثير من معاني المجاز والكنايات عن أن تكون مدلولاً التزاميا .
ومَن عبّر به كصاحب «التلخيص» فإنَّه قال : وشرطه اللُّزوم الذهني، ولو لاعتقاد المخاطب
و : «التَّغَيُّرِ» عَلَى «الحُدُوثِ»، وَ: «الأَثَرِ» عَلَى وُجُودِ المُؤَفِّرِ». أَمَّا الأَوَّلَانِ : فَلِأَنَّ العَقْلَ يُجَوِّزُ كَوْنَ الغُرَابِ أَبْيَضَ وَالتَّلْجِ أَزْرَقَ مَثَلاً ، وَإِذَا قَطَعْتَ النَّظَرَ عَمَّا فِي الخَارِجِ وَرَجِعْتَ إِلَى نَفْسِ حَقِيقَتِهِمَا ، عَلِمْتَ انْتِفَاءَ اللُّزُوم . وَأَمَّا الأَخِيرَانِ : فَلِأَنَّ العَقْلَ لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا تَصَوَّرَ التَّغَيُّرَ وَالأَثَرَ عَلِمَ الحُدُوثَ وَالمُؤَثْرَ، بَلْ يَتَصَوُّرُ الأَوَّلَيْنِ ذَاهِلاً عَنِ الأَخِيرَيْنِ ، أَوْ جَاهِلاً بِلُزُومِهِمَا لَهُمَا، وَهَذَا مَعْنَى عَدَمِ اللُّزُومِ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ العَقَلُ بَعْدَ الِاسْتِدْلَالِ يَقْطَعُ بِاللُّزُومِ فِي الخَارِجِ، وَلَا يُجَوِّزُ انْتِفَاءَهُ.
قوله : (وَ : التَّغَوُّرِ» عَلَى الحُدُوثِ) تبع هذا الهلالي على عادته وفيه نظر، بل ما تصورت التّغير إلَّا وتصورت معه الحدوث، ولذلك جعله المناطقة في الأقيسة دالا على الحدوث، والموجود في كلام السَّنوسي وغيره : ( والأجرام على الحدوث، وهو حقٌّ . والظاهر أنَّ ما ذكره الشارح من كون لزوم الحدوث للتَّغير ليس ذهنيًّا إنَّما هو باعتبار الأشخاص والأحوال؛ بدليل أنه قال بعده: «نعم؛ إذا صار اللُّزوم في الثاني بديهيا». قوله : (وَلَا يُجَوِّزُ انْتِفَاءَهُ) أي: كما يجوزه في بياض الثلج وسواد الغراب، فالانتفاء الذي لا يجوزه العقل حينئذ هو انتفاء اللُّزوم في الخارج. وأما انتفاؤه في الذهن فالعقل يجوزه؛ بمعنى: أنَّه بعد العلم باللزوم بين الدَّال والمدلول يجوز العقل حصول الدَّال في العقل بدون حصول المدلول فيه، وإن كان لا ينفكُ خارجاً؛ هذا معنى قولهم : اللُّزوم الذهني هو أن يكون المعنى كلما فهم فهم لازمه
Types of conventional indication. And this necessary concomitant is called 'the absolute concomitant'. Or it accompanies it in the mind only, not externally, like 'sight' indicated by the word 'blindness'. For whenever blindness is imagined in the mind, sight is imagined with it; because the meaning of blindness is nothing but the absence of sight from that which is capable of seeing. So sight is a concomitant of it mentally, while it is incompatible with it externally. And sight is not indicated by blindness by way of inclusion; for blindness is not the absence and sight such that sight is part of its meaning. Rather, its meaning is the absence related to sight, and the thing to which it is related is outside the relation. As for the indication of the word to a meaning external to its meaning that is concomitant with it externally but not mentally, it is not called in logic 'indication of concomitance', but it is called by that name in the two sciences of uṣūl and bayān. That is like the indication of the word 'crow' for 'blackness', and 'snow' for 'whiteness', and 'change' for 'temporal origination', and 'trace' for the existence of the effecter. As for the first two: because the mind can imagine the crow being white and the snow being blue, for example. And if you disregard what is in the external and return to their very essences, you know the absence of concomitance. As for the last two: because the mind does not whenever it imagines change and trace know temporal origination and the effecter. Rather, it imagines the first two while being heedless of the last two, or ignorant of their concomitance for them. And this is the meaning of the absence of concomitance in the mind, even though the mind after inference is certain of the concomitance in the external and does not consider its absence possible.
His saying: 'the absolute concomitant' is named 'absolute' because its concomitance is not restricted to the mind or to the external. His saying: 'sight is imagined with it' means its concept, which is the power of sight that is capable of perceiving what is imprinted in it from the origins of the forms of bodies.
His saying: 'and sight is not' – the origin of this is from al-Quṭb in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya, and its text: For you might say: sight is part of the concept of blindness, so its indication of it is by inclusion, not by concomitance. We say: blindness is the absence of sight, not the absence and sight.
His saying: 'the absence related to sight' means the relation, not the thing related, but the relation must be considered; otherwise its concept would be absolute absence, which is false. So the relation is considered without the thing related. His saying: 'and it is called by that name in the two sciences of uṣūl and bayān': because of that, many benefits are derived from the Book and the Sunnah and the words of the imams. And if they had required mental concomitance, many meanings of metaphor and metonymy would be excluded from being concomitant indications.
And whoever expressed it like the author of al-Talkhīṣ, for he said: its condition is mental concomitance, even if it is by the belief of the addressee.
And 'change' for 'temporal origination', and 'trace' for the existence of the effecter. As for the first two: because the mind can imagine the crow being white and the snow being blue... etc. (then repeats the same paragraph with slight variation). His saying: 'and change for temporal origination' – al-Hilālī followed this in his usual manner, and there is consideration of it. Rather, I do not imagine change except that I imagine with it temporal origination, and that is why the logicians in syllogisms made it indicative of temporal origination. And what is found in the speech of al-Sanūsī and others: 'and the celestial bodies indicate temporal origination', and that is true. And it appears that what the commentator mentioned, that the concomitance of temporal origination for change is not mental, is only with respect to persons and states; as evidence that he said afterwards: 'yes, when the concomitance in the second becomes self-evident'. His saying: 'and does not consider its absence possible' means just as he considers it possible in the whiteness of snow and blackness of the crow. So the absence that the mind does not consider possible then is the absence of concomitance in the external. As for its absence in the mind, the mind considers it possible; in the sense that after knowledge of the concomitance between the signifier and signified, the mind can conceive the signifier occurring in the mind without the signified occurring in it, even if it does not separate externally. This is the meaning of their saying: mental concomitance is that whenever the meaning is understood, its necessary concomitant is understood.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage defines the types of conventional indication (دلالة وضعية) with a focus on the necessary concomitant (لازم). It begins by introducing the 'absolute concomitant' (اللازم المطلق) which is not restricted to mental or external concomitance. Then it explains mental concomitance (لزوم ذهني) using the example of 'blindness' and 'sight': whenever blindness is imagined, sight is necessarily imagined because blindness is defined as the absence of sight. The author clarifies that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness (which would be تضمن), but rather it is a mental concomitant. Next, the passage distinguishes between mental and external concomitance: external concomitance (لزوم خارجي) without mental concomitance is not considered 'indication of concomitance' (دلالة التزام) in logic, but it is in uṣūl and bayān. Examples are given: 'crow' for 'blackness' and 'snow' for 'whiteness' are not mental concomitants because the mind can conceive of a white crow or blue snow, so the concomitance is not necessary in the mind. In contrast, 'change' for 'temporal origination' and 'trace' for 'effecter' are also not mental concomitants because one can think of change without thinking of temporal origination (e.g., a child may not know the connection). However, after inference, the mind does recognize the external concomitance. The super-commentator (Bannani) then refines the discussion: he notes that the term 'absolute concomitant' is unrestricted. He addresses a subtle point regarding 'blindness' and 'sight', citing al-Quṭb's commentary on al-Shamsiyya, where it is argued that sight is not part of the meaning of blindness; rather, the meaning is 'absence related to sight'. The relation is considered, but not the thing related. The super-commentator also explains why uṣūl and bayān allow external concomitance: to derive many benefits from texts, otherwise metaphor and metonymy would not be valid concomitant indications. Finally, he critiques al-Hilālī's claim that the concomitance of 'change' to 'temporal origination' is not mental, arguing that it actually is a self-evident mental concomitance for many, and the commentator's view may be relative to persons and states.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim): A necessary concomitant; a meaning that is necessarily implied by another meaning either mentally (ذهنا) or externally (خارجا), or both. [Note: In uṣūl and bayān, external concomitance is accepted as دلالة التزام, whereas in logic (mantiq) only mental concomitance is considered.]
دلالة التزام (dalālat iltizām): Indication by necessary concomitance, where the word indicates a meaning outside its literal meaning that is necessarily associated with it in the mind. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
لزوم ذهني (luzūm dhihnī): Mental concomitance: whenever the meaning of the word is understood, its necessary concomitant is also understood in the mind, regardless of external reality. [Note: Some later scholars (e.g., al-Talkhīṣ) include belief of the addressee, broadening the condition.]
لزوم خارجي (luzūm khārijī): External concomitance: the concomitant meaning exists in reality but is not necessarily understood in the mind when the word is uttered. [Note: In logic, this is not considered دلالة التزام; in uṣūl and bayān it is accepted.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bannānī, why might the indication of 'change' for 'temporal origination' not qualify as mental concomitance for some individuals?
A) Because change and temporal origination are not connected in reality.
B) Because some people are ignorant of the connection or do not think of it when hearing the word.
C) Because temporal origination is not a necessary concomitant of change.
D) Because the mind always conceives temporal origination when thinking of change.

Key Takeaway
The logical indication of necessary concomitance requires that the mind immediately grasps the connection; this condition distinguishes logic from uṣūl and bayān, which accept external concomitance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The trace indicates the existence of the effecter.' Using the criteria from this lesson, determine whether this is a case of mental concomitance or external concomitance in logic. Explain your reasoning.
?
In the science of uṣūl al-fiqh, the word 'lion' can indicate a brave man by metaphorical extension. According to this lesson, would logic accept this as دلالة التزام? Why or why not? (Hint: consider the condition of mental concomitance.)

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Two Schools on the Status of Tadammun and Iltizām
Classification of the three indications in relation to convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main opinions regarding whether tadammun and iltizām are conventional (wadʿiyya) or intellectual (ʿaqliyya), and cite the key thinkers associated with each.

Core Idea
The dispute over the three indications is a dispute over how much convention alone can convey.

In this passage, the commentator presents two competing views on the relation of tadammun (containment) and iltizām (implication) to linguistic convention (wadʿ). One view considers convention as the direct cause for mutābaqa and an indirect cause for the others; the other view considers the latter two as purely intellectual, relying on reason after convention. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how words communicate meaning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three indications
List the three types of dalāla: mutābaqa (correspondence), tadammun (containment), iltizām (implication). Note their definitions from the passage.
Mutābaqa: word indicates its entire meaning; tadammun: word indicates a part; iltizām: word indicates something external but necessary.
2
Extract the causal relationship in the first opinion
According to the first opinion, wadʿ is a cause (sabab) for mutābaqa, and a cause of a cause (sabab sabab) for tadammun and iltizām. This means convention directly gives the whole meaning, and the part and necessary follow indirectly.
The passage calls this the opinion of most logicians (jumhūr al-mantiqa).
3
Extract the causal relationship in the second opinion
The second opinion says tadammun and iltizām are intellectual (ʿaqliyyatān), meaning their cause is not convention but the intellect understanding the whole or the necessary. This was held by the author of al-Miftāḥ, al-Qazwīnī, and al-Subkī.
The passage notes this opinion is based on a particular definition of 'conventional indication'.
4
Compare the consequences
In the first opinion, all three indications are ultimately conventional because they depend on convention directly or indirectly. In the second, only mutābaqa is conventional; the other two depend on reason after convention.
The commentator later shows the dispute may be verbal.

وَوَجْهُ نِسْبَةِ الدَّلَالَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ إِلَى الوَضْعِ : (۱) - أَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ فِي الأَوْلَى، وَسَبَبُ سَبَبٍ فِي الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ (۱) - (۲) - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ عَقْلِيَّتَانِ ؛ نَظَراً إِلَى أَنَّ سَبَبَهُمَا هُوَ فَهُمُ الكُلِّ وَالمَلْزُومُ . قوله : (وَوَجْهُ نِسْبَةِ الدَّلَالَاتِ الثَّلَاثِ إِلَى الوَضْعِ : أَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ فِي الأُولَى... إلخ) بحث فيه اليوسي في حواشي المختصر : بأنَّ الوضع ليس هو السَّببُ التَّامُّ للمطابقة؛ لتوقفها على أمرين آخرين هما : العلم بالوضع، وخطور الفظ بالبال. وأجاب بجوابين أحسنهما : أنَّه لم يعتبرهما لاشتراط الدَّلالات الثَّلاث فيهما، فالتفصيل المذكور إذن بعد وجودهما . قوله : (وَسَبَبُ سَبَبٍ... إلخ) هذا لا يـنـاسـب مـخـتـاره مـن أنـه أنـه لا انتقال؛ إذِ السَّبب يتقدَّم على المسبّب، وفهم الكلِّ على أنَّه لا انتقال ليس سابقاً على فهم الجزء ؛ اللَّهمَّ إلَّا أن يجاب: بأنَّ المراد بالصَّبيَّة اللُّزوم؛ أي: الوضع سبب في فهم المعنى، وفهم المعنى يلزم فهم الجزء. قوله : (وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ عَقْلِيَّتَانِ على هذا القول دَرج صاحب المفتاح»، وتبعه القزويني في «التلخيص، وكذا السبكي في جمع الجوامع، وعلى القول الأول أكثر المناطقة كما في «زكريا»، وهو مبني على أنَّ المراد بـ اللفظية الوضعيّة : فهم المعنى مِنَ اللفظ الموضوع؛ كان بواسطة أم لا . سَوَاءٌ فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. قوله : (سَوَاءٌ فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ أي : سواءٌ وُضع للكلِّ لفظ أم لا، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّه لا يجب لكلِّ معنى لفظ ؛ ضرورة بقاء كثيرٍ مِنَ المعاني كأنواع الرَّوائح مثلاً لا ألفاظ لها، وإنَّما تعرف بالإضافة كـ: رائحة كذا وكذا : أنواع الطعوم والألم . قوله : (وَقِيلَ : دَلَالَةُ التَّضَمُّنِ وَضْعِيَّةٌ ... إلخ) على هذا القول المحققون مِنَ الأصوليين كالآمدي وابن الحاجب والعضد ؛ لأنَّ الفهم في الدَّلالتين واحد، فإن اعتبر بالنسبة إلى كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الجزأين سميت الدَّلالة تضمناً»، وإن اعتبر بالنسبة إلى المجموع سميت: «مطابقة»، وأمَّا السُّبكي في جمع الجوامع فقد تبع البيانيين في اصطلاحهم. قوله : (وَالخِلافُ لَفْظِيٌّ هذا ما حقَّقه الشَّيخ أبو عبد الله الشَّريف التلمساني شارح «الجمل»، وذلك إن عنينا بـ الدَّلالة) الوضعية : أنَّها التي تتوقف على الوضع؛ سواء كان الوضع كافياً في فهم المعنى أم لا ، فالتّضمن والالتزام وضعيّتان. وإن عنينا بـ «الدَّلالة الوضعية»: ما يكون الوضع فيه كافياً في فهم المعنى، فالدَّلالتان عقليَّتان.
And the aspect of relating the three indications to convention (wadʿ): (1) That it (convention) is a cause in the first [mutābaqa], and a cause of a cause in the latter two. (2) And it was said that the latter two are intellectual (ʿaqliyyatan), considering that their cause is understanding the whole and the necessary. His statement: 'And the aspect of relating the three indications to convention: that it is a cause in the first, etc.' - Yusī commented in his notes on al-Mukhtaṣar that convention is not the complete cause for mutābaqa because it depends on two other things: knowledge of the convention and the occurrence of the word in the mind. He answered with two answers, the best of which is that he did not consider them because the three indications require them both; thus the mentioned detail is after their existence. His statement: 'And a cause of a cause, etc.' - This does not match his choice that there is no transition, for the cause precedes the effect, and understanding the whole as entailing no transition is not prior to understanding the part. Unless it is answered that by 'causality' is meant necessity: i.e., convention is a cause for understanding the meaning, and understanding the meaning necessitates understanding the part. His statement: 'And it was said that the latter two are intellectual' - According to this opinion, the author of al-Miftāḥ proceeded, and al-Qazwīnī followed him in al-Talkhīṣ, and likewise al-Subkī in Jamʿ al-Jawāmiʿ. The first opinion is held by most logicians as in [the commentary of] Zakariyyā, and it is based on that what is meant by 'verbal conventional' is understanding the meaning from the conventional word, whether by means of it or not. Whether understood from the word or from other than it. His statement: 'Whether understood from the word or from other than it' - i.e., whether a word is placed for the whole or not. It contains an indication that it is not necessary that every meaning has a word; necessarily many meanings such as types of smells, for example, have no words, but are known by addition, like 'the smell of such-and-such', and types of tastes and pain. His statement: 'And it was said: the indication of containment (tadammun) is conventional, etc.' - According to this opinion are the verifiers among uṣūliyyūn like al-Āmidī, Ibn al-Ḥājib, and al-ʿAḍud, because the understanding in the two indications is one: if considered with respect to each of the two parts, the indication is called 'containment', and if considered with respect to the whole, it is called 'correspondence'. As for al-Subkī in Jamʿ al-Jawāmiʿ, he followed the rhetoricians in their terminology. His statement: 'And the dispute is verbal' - This is what Shaykh Abū ʿAbd Allāh al-Sharīf al-Tilimsānī, commentator of al-Jumal, verified, because if we mean by 'conventional indication' that which depends on convention, whether convention is sufficient for understanding the meaning or not, then containment and implication are both conventional. And if we mean by 'conventional indication' that in which convention is sufficient for understanding the meaning, then both indications are intellectual.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an extensive commentary on a line from al-Sullam regarding how the three types of indication (dalāla) relate to linguistic convention (wadʿ). The author presents two initial opinions: (1) convention is a cause for the first (mutābaqa, correspondence) and a cause of a cause for the latter two (tadammun, containment; iltizām, implication); (2) the latter two are purely intellectual, depending only on understanding the whole or the necessary. Then the commentator discusses an objection by Yusī in his glosses on al-Mukhtaṣar, arguing that convention is not the complete cause even for mutābaqa because it requires knowledge of the convention and recalling the word. The best answer is that these are presupposed by all three indications. Next, a problem is raised: calling convention a 'cause of a cause' implies a temporal priority that conflicts with the view that there is no transition (intiqāl) in understanding; the solution is to interpret 'causality' as logical consequence (luzūm). The commentary then notes that the second opinion (latter two are intellectual) was followed by the author of al-Miftāḥ and others, while the first opinion is that of most logicians. The distinction hinges on whether 'conventional indication' means understanding from the word directly or including mediated understanding. The author explains that it does not require a word for every meaning; many meanings are known by description. Finally, the passage presents the third and fourth opinions: some uṣūlī scholars consider containment conventional (since the part is included in the topic), while implication is intellectual (since the necessary is outside); others say the dispute is verbal, as Shaykh al-Tilimsānī resolved: if convention alone suffices, both are conventional; if convention must fully suffice, both are intellectual. Thus the passage thoroughly examines the classifications of the three indications.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the first opinion regarding the relation of the three indications to convention?
A) Convention is a cause for each of the three equally.
B) Convention is a cause for mutābaqa, and a cause of a cause for the other two.
C) Convention is irrelevant for tadammun and iltizām.
D) Convention is only a cause for tadammun.

Key Takeaway
The two opinions on the nature of tadammun and iltizām hinge on whether convention directly suffices for understanding them, or whether reason must intervene.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'zakāh' in the Quran. Identify what each of the three indications would convey for this term (mutābaqa: the whole obligation; tadammun: specific conditions like niṣāb; iltizām: leading to purification of wealth). Then, based on the two opinions, determine whether you would classify tadammun and iltizām as conventional or intellectual for this example.

What Is Mantiq?

Resolving the Dispute: The Role of Convention in the Three Indications
Refinements and resolution of the dispute on the three indications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the key objections and refinements to the two main opinions, including Yusī's criticism, the transition problem, and the verbal resolution by al-Tilimsānī.

Core Idea
The apparent disagreement over the three indications dissolves when the definition of 'conventional indication' is clarified.

After presenting the two main opinions, the commentator engages with critical objections. Yusī argues that convention is not even a complete cause for mutābaqa, and the 'cause of a cause' phrase seems to imply a temporal transition that contradicts the view of immediate understanding. Finally, al-Tilimsānī resolves that the dispute is merely verbal, depending on how one defines 'conventional indication.' This lesson walks through these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Understand Yusī's objection
Yusī notes that for mutābaqa, convention alone is not sufficient; one also needs knowledge of the convention and the word occurring in the mind. Thus convention is not a complete cause.
The commentator gives the best answer: these two conditions are presupposed for all three indications, so they do not undermine the causal role.
2
Analyze the 'cause of a cause' problem
The phrase 'cause of a cause' suggests a temporal sequence: convention causes understanding of the whole, which then causes understanding of the part. But the author of al-Sullam holds there is no transition (lā intiqāl). How to reconcile?
The solution: interpret 'causality' as logical necessity (luzūm), not temporal causation. Convention causes understanding of the whole, and understanding the whole necessitates understanding the part without temporal gap.
3
Examine the verbal resolution
Al-Tilimsānī shows that the dispute is over the definition of 'conventional indication.' If it means 'depends on convention (whether sufficient or not),' then all three are conventional. If it means 'convention alone is sufficient,' then only mutābaqa is conventional.
This resolves the apparent disagreement: the two opinions are not contradictory but are based on different definitions.

And the aspect of relating the three indications to convention (wadʿ): (1) That it (convention) is a cause in the first [mutābaqa], and a cause of a cause in the latter two. What Is Mantiq?

Al-Qarafi's Objection on the Exclusivity of the Three Indications
Author presents al-Qarafī's objection that general term's indication is outside the three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conventional indication and explain why the indication of a general term on its individuals is not covered by any of them.

Core Idea
The general term's indication on its individuals is the hidden fourth type that challenges the threefold classification.

In this lesson, we examine a critical objection raised by the Mālikī jurist al-Qarafī against the classical division of conventional indication (dalāla waḍʿiyya) into only three kinds: correspondence (muṭābaqa), containment (taḍammun), and implication (iltizām). Al-Qarafī argues that the way a general term like 'scholars' indicates each of its individual members falls outside all three categories, calling into question whether the classification is exhaustive. Understanding this objection is crucial for mastering the theory of indication in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of conventional indication
Muṭābaqa: the word indicates its full meaning; taḍammun: indicates a part of the meaning; iltizām: indicates a necessary concomitant outside the meaning. Write these down mentally.
2
Define the general term (al-ʿāmm)
In Islamic logic, al-ʿāmm is a word that encompasses all individuals that are suitable for it without restriction. Example: 'scholars' indicates every scholar, 'polytheists' every polytheist.
See the passage's definition using Quran 9:5.
3
Apply the three types to the general term's indication
Does 'scholars' indicate a particular scholar by muṭābaqa? No, because muṭābaqa would require the full meaning (the set of all scholars). By taḍammun? No, because the individual scholar is not a conceptual part of the meaning 'scholars' (the meaning is the set, not an individual). By iltizām? No, because the individual is not an external necessary concomitant.
4
Articulate al-Qarafī's conclusion
Since the indication of a general term on some of its individuals is not covered by any of the three, the classification is incomplete. This is a challenge to the standard doctrine.
The passage does not include the response; later scholars may provide answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِيهَانِ: (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : أَوْرَدَ القَرَافِيُّ عَلَى حَصْرِ الدَّلَالَةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ فِي الثَّلَاثِ : أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ العَامِ عَلَى بَعْضٍ أَفْرَادِهِ خَارِجَةٌ عَنْ جَمِيعِهَا . قوله : (أَوْرَدَ القَرَافِيُّ هو العلامة شهاب الدين أبو العباس أحمد بن إدريس الصنهاجي المصري القرافي، انتهت إليه رئاسة المالكيَّة في عصره، وبرع في الفقه وأصوله ، ألَّف التصانيف الشهيرة ك: «الذَّخيرة»، و«القوانين»، و«شرح المحصول»، و«التنقيح وشرحه، توفّي في جمادى الأخيرة سنة أربع وثمانين وست مئة، ودفن بالقرافة، وقد صرّح بهذا الإشكال أيضاً السهروردي وغيره. قوله : (أَنَّ دَلَالَةَ العَامُ) هو لفظ يستغرق الصَّالح له من غير حصر كـ لفظ «العلماء» في قولنا : لا تؤذ العلماء»، ولفظ «المشركين في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٥] .
Two notices: (1) The first: al-Qarafī objected to the restriction of conventional indication to three, that the indication of the general term upon some of its individuals is outside all of them. His statement: 'al-Qarafī' – he is the scholar Shihāb al-Dīn Abū al-ʿAbbās Aḥmad ibn Idrīs al-Ṣanhājī al-Miṣrī al-Qarafī; the leadership of the Malikī school in his time ended with him; he excelled in fiqh and its principles. He authored famous works like al-Dhakhīra, al-Qawānīn, Sharḥ al-Maḥṣūl, and al-Tanqīḥ and its commentary. He died in Jumādā al-Ākhira 684 AH and was buried in al-Qarāfa. Also al-Suhrawardī and others explicitly stated this problem. His statement: 'That the indication of the general term' – al-ʿām is a word that encompasses all that it is suitable for without limitation, like the word 'scholars' in our saying 'Do not harm scholars', and the word 'polytheists' in Allah's verse [Quran 9:5] 'Kill the polytheists.'
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a tanbīh (notice) raising a critical objection against the well-known classification of conventional indication into only three types (mutābaqa, taḍammun, iltizām). The objection, attributed to the eminent Mālikī jurist al-Qarafī, states that the indication of a general term (al-ʿām) upon some of its individuals is not covered by any of the three. The commentator then provides a brief biography of al-Qarafī and defines the term 'general' (al-ʿām) with Quranic and juristic examples. The objection is that when you say 'scholars', it indicates each individual scholar, but this is not mutābaqa (which requires the full meaning), nor taḍammun (since the individual is not a conceptual part), nor iltizām (since the individual is not an external necessary concomitant). This challenges the completeness of the triple classification. The author does not provide the answer in this passage; it may be addressed in the second notice or later.

العام
حصر الدلالة الوضعية

B) Because the individual is neither the full meaning (mutabaqa), nor a part (tadammun), nor a necessary concomitant (iltizam).
C) Because the general term is ambiguous and thus has no clear indication.
D) Because the indication is based on logical entailment, not convention.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qarafī's objection reveals that the indication of a general term on its individuals is not covered by muṭābaqa, taḍammun, or iltizām, challenging the exhaustiveness of the threefold classification.

Why Logic Teaches Compound Before Simple Terms
Author explains why compound terms are introduced before simple terms in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptual (mafhum) and referential (masduq) priority and explain why compound terms are introduced before simple terms in logic.

Core Idea
Conceptual priority determines teaching order: the simple is a negation of the compound.

In this passage, the commentator defends the decision to teach compound terms before simple terms in logic. An objector claims the simple should come first because it is a part of the compound. The response reveals a crucial distinction between the concept (mafhum) and the referent (masduq) of a term, a distinction that is foundational for understanding definitions and divisions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
What is the author trying to prove? He claims that compound terms should be taught before simple terms in logic. Find the rationale he gives.
2
Understand the rationale
The author says the concept of a compound is existential (wujudiyy), while the concept of a simple is non-existential (adamiyy) because it is a negation. Since negation requires prior understanding of what is negated, the compound must come first.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector points out that the simple is a part of the compound and a part precedes the whole. Also, if the simple is a part, how can it be a co-division of the compound? Two problems: circularity (dawr) and co-division distinctness.
4
Examine the resolution
The answer distinguishes between referent (masduq) and concept (mafhum). The part-whole relationship holds in terms of referent, while the co-division relationship holds in terms of concept. The simple's concept is a negation of the compound's concept, so conceptually they are distinct and opposite. No contradiction.
5
Apply the analogy
The commentator notes that the same distinction applies to the division of knowledge into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Concept is part of assent yet is treated as a co-division, reinforcing the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ (۲) - الثَّانِي : قَدَّمَ النَّاظِمُ المُرَكَّبَ وَتَعْرِيفَهُ عَلَى المُفْرَد؛ لأَنَّ التَّعْرِيفَ لِلمَفْهُوم، وَمَنْهُومُ المُرَكَّبِ وُجُودِيٌّ ، وَمَنْهُومُ المُفْرَدِ عَدَمِيٌّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ سُلِبَ فِيهِ مَا أُثْبِتَ فِي المُرَكَّبِ، وَلَا يُعْقَلُ سَلْبُ أَمْرٍ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقُلِ الأَمْرِ المَسْلُوبِ.

لا يُقَالُ : المُفْرَدُ جُزْءُ المُرَكَّب، وَالجُزْءُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى الكُلِّ ، وَأَيْضاً : الجُزْءُ لَازِمٌ لِلْكُلِّ ضَرُورَةٌ، فَكَيْفَ يُجْعَلُ قَسِيماً لَهُ، وَقَسِيمُ الشَّيْء يُبَاينه لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: كَوْنُهُ جُزْءاً مِنَ المُرَكَبِ وَلَازماً لَهُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِحَسَبِ ذَاتِهِمَا ؛ أَيْ : مَصْدُوقِهِمَا ، وَذَلِكَ لَا يُنَافِي التَّقَابُلَ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَالتَّبايُنُ بِحَسَبِ مَفْهُومِهِمَا المُقْتَضِي تَأخُرَ ذِكْرِ المُفْرَدِ عَنِ المُرَكَّبِ، فَافْهَمْ.

قوله : (لَا يُقَالُ حاصل السُّؤال : أنَّه يلزم على ما درج عليه النَّاظم أمران: الدور، وكون قسم الشَّيء يُباينُهُ

بيانُ الأوّل: أنَّ الجزء سابق على الكلِّ، فيكون الكل متوقفاً عليه فلو انعكس الأمر وكان المفرد متوقفاً على المركَّب كما ذكرتم ، لَزِمَ الدَّورُ .

وأمَّا الثَّاني: فظاهر.

وحاصلُ الجواب : أنَّ الجهة منفكَّةٌ، فإنَّ المفرد يتوقف على المركَّب من حيث المفهوم؛ أي : ما لا يدلُّ جزؤه، والمركَّب يتوقف عليه من حيث المصدوق، وكذلك تقابلهما من حيث المفهوم، وكون الجزء لازماً للكلِّ من حيث المصدوق.

وبالجملة كلِّ مِنَ المفرد والمركَّب له مفهوم و مصدوق، ومصدوق المركب ـ أي : ذاته ـ ؛ ك: «زيد قائم ونحوه متأخر عن مصدوق المفرد کـ: «زید» و«عمرو» ونحوهما، ومفهوم المركب ما دلّ جزؤه. .. إلخ متقدم على مفهوم المفرد، وهو ما لا يدلُّ جزوه؛ لأنَّ الثَّاني سلبٌ وهو للأوَّل، وسلبُ الشَّيء فرح عن وجوده ؛ قال الهلالي : وهذا البحث مثله يوردُ في تقسيم العلم إلى التَّصوُّر والتَّصديق؛ لأنَّ التَّصوُّر جزءٌ مِنَ التَّصديق عند الإمام، ولازم له عند غيره، ومع ذلك جعل قسيماً له، وجوابه ما ذكر آنفاً .
Section on Investigations of Expressions. (2) The second: The poet (al-Sullam) presented the compound and its definition before the simple, because the definition is of the concept, and the concept of the compound is existential, and the concept of the simple is non-existential, because in it is negated what was affirmed in the compound, and the negation of a thing is not understood except after understanding the negated thing. It is not to be said: The simple is a part of the compound, and the part precedes the whole, and also: the part is necessarily concomitant with the whole, so how is it made a co-division of it, while the co-division of a thing is distinct from it? Because we say: Its being a part of the compound and concomitant with it is only in respect of their essences, i.e., their referents, and that does not contradict the opposition between them, and the distinction is in respect of their concepts which necessitate the later mention of the simple after the compound, so understand. His statement: “It is not to be said”: The summary of the objection: that what follows from the poet’s procedure are two matters: circularity, and that the division of a thing is distinct from it. Explanation of the first: that the part precedes the whole, so the whole depends on it; if the matter were reversed and the simple depended on the compound as you mentioned, circularity would result. As for the second, it is obvious. The summary of the answer: that the respect is disjunctive, for the simple depends on the compound in terms of concept, i.e., that which its part does not indicate, and the compound depends on it in terms of referent, and likewise their opposition is in terms of concept, and the part’s being concomitant with the whole is in terms of referent. In summary, each of the simple and the compound has a concept and a referent. The referent of the compound, i.e., its essence, like “Zayd standing” and similar, is posterior to the referent of the simple, like “Zayd” and “Amr” and similar. And the concept of the compound, which is what its part indicates, etc., is prior to the concept of the simple, which is what its part does not indicate, because the second is a negation of the first, and the negation of a thing is a branch of its existence. Al-Hilali said: This discussion is similar to that raised in the division of knowledge into concept and assent, because concept is a part of assent according to the Imam, and necessary for it according to others, yet he made it a co-division of it, and its answer is what was mentioned above.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange within a commentary on al-Sullam. The author begins by stating the rationale for presenting compound terms before simple terms in logic: the concept of a compound (murakkab) is existential (wujudiyy), while the concept of a simple (mufrad) is non-existential (adamiyy), being a negation of what was affirmed in the compound. Since negation cannot be understood without first understanding the negated thing, the compound must be introduced first. An objection is then raised: the simple is a part of the compound, and a part precedes the whole; also, a part necessarily implies the whole, so how can the simple be made a co-division of the compound, since co-divisions are mutually distinct? The answer clarifies that the part-whole relationship holds in terms of referent (masduq), i.e., real existence, while the conceptual (mafhum) relationship involves opposition and distinction. The compound's concept is prior because it is positive, while the simple's concept is negative; thus, the compound is taught first. The commentary then elaborates on the two problems (circularity and co-division distinctness) and provides a response: the simple conceptually depends on the compound, while the compound referentially depends on the simple. An analogy is drawn to the division of knowledge into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), where concept is a part of assent yet is considered a co-division, a similar case resolved by the same principle.

Key Terms
المُرَكَّب
المُفْرَد
المفهوم
المصدوق
الدَّور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author present the compound before the simple term?
A) Because the compound is more common in usage.
B) Because the concept of the simple is a negation of the concept of the compound and requires prior understanding of the compound.
C) Because the simple is a part of the compound in reality, so the compound must be introduced after the parts.
D) Because the compound is defined by the simple.

Key Takeaway
The order of teaching terms in logic is determined by conceptual priority: the simple's concept is a negation of the compound's, so the compound must be introduced first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the terms 'action' (fi'l) and 'obligation' (wujub). In fiqh, obligation is a quality of an action. Is the concept of obligation prior to the concept of action, or vice versa? Use the mafhum/masduq distinction to argue which should be defined first in a fiqh primer.
