Distinguishing 'Alam al-Jins and Ism al-Jins
Shah al-Bannani explains the two opinions and differences between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins according to the two opinions and identify the key differences.

Core Idea
'Alam al-jins is a proper noun for a genus with a mental covenant; ism al-jins is a common noun for the quiddity.

This passage from al-Bannani's commentary clarifies a subtle distinction that often confuses students. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting Arabic texts in logic and usul al-fiqh, where generic terms carry different implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions on ism al-jins
First, locate the two opinions about what ism al-jins signifies. The first opinion says it signifies the quiddity with the condition of existence in an unspecified individual. The second opinion says it signifies the quiddity as such, without any condition of individuality.
The first opinion is attributed to al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-Subki, and Sa'd al-Din.
2
Extract the differences between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins under the first opinion
According to the first opinion, there are two differences: (1) 'Alam al-jins disregards referents, while ism al-jins requires existence in one unspecified individual. (2) 'Alam al-jins indicates a mental covenant (the reality is present to the addressee), while ism al-jins does not.
The term 'mental covenant' refers to the reality being already known and present in the mind.
3
Extract the difference under the second opinion
Under the second opinion, the difference is only one: both are posited for the quiddity without individuals, but 'alam al-jins adds the condition of indicating a pre-known reality (mental presence), while ism al-jins does not.
4
Understand the scope of the disagreement
The disagreement applies only to the indefinite generic noun. The definite generic noun (with the definiteness particle) is equivalent in meaning to 'alam al-jins. Also, if 'alam al-jins is considered definite only in wording, then it is synonymous with ism al-jins.
This final point is from Ibn Malik and al-Radi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِاعْتِبَارِ وُجُودِهَا فِي أَفْرَادِهَا الْخَارِجِيَّةِ أَيْ : فَيَكُونُ اسْمُ الْجِنْسِ مَوْضُوعًا لِلْمَاهِيَةِ وُحْدَهَا لَا بِعَيْنِهَا وَتُسَمَّى فَرْدًا مُبْهَمًا، وَفَرْدًا مُنْتَشِرًا، وَوُحْدَةً شَائِعَةً، وَهَذَا الْقَوْلُ لِلْآمِدِيِّ وَابْنِ الْحَاجِبِ وَالسُّبْكِيِّ وَسَعْدِ الدِّينِ. وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ اسْمَ الْجِنْسِ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْمَاهِيَةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هِيَ هِيَ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُهُ فِي الْفَرْدِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ لِتَحَقُّقِ الْمَاهِيَةِ فِيهِ وَكَوْنِهِ حَاصِلًا لَهَا، وَهُوَ اسْتِعْمَالٌ حَقِيقِيٌّ لَا مَجَازِيٌّ ; لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ مُسْتَعْمَلٌ فِي الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَالْفَرْدِيَّةُ مُسْتَفَادَةٌ مِنْ خَارِجٍ. فَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ عَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ وَاسْمِهِ عَلَى الْقَوْلِ الْأَوَّلِ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ: أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنَّ عَلَمَ الْجِنْسِ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْمَاهِيَةِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ مُصَدَّقَاتِهَا، وَاسْمُ الْجِنْسِ لَهَا بِقَيْدِ وُجُودِهَا فِي وَاحِدٍ لَا بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ مِنَ الْمُصَدَّقَاتِ. ثَانِيهِمَا : أَنَّ عَلَمَ الْجِنْسِ يَدُلُّ بِجَوْهَرِ لَفْظِهِ عَلَى كَوْنِ تِلْكَ الْحَقِيقَةِ مَعْهُودَةً فِي ذِهْنِ الْمُخَاطَبِ حَاضِرَةً فِيهِ مُتَصَوَّرَةً لَهُ وَاسْمُ الْجِنْسِ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى عَهْدٍ أَصْلًا. وَأَمَّا عَلَى الْقَوْلِ الثَّانِي فَمِنْ وَجْهٍ وَاحِدٍ وَهُوَ : أَنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا وَإِنْ كَانَ مَوْضُوعًا لِلْمَاهِيَةِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ أَفْرَادِهَا، لَكِنْ فِي عَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ زِيَادَةُ قَيْدٍ، وَهُوَ الدَّلَالَةُ بِجَوْهَرِ لَفْظِهِ عَلَى كَوْنِ تِلْكَ الْحَقِيقَةِ مَعْهُودَةً مَعْلُومَةً لِلْمُخَاطَبِ، وَاسْمُ الْجِنْسِ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تِلْكَ إِلَّا بِالْأَدَاةِ إِنْ كَانَتْ. وَالْخِلَافُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي مَوْضُوعِ اسْمِ الْجِنْسِ الْمُنَكَّرِ، وَأَمَّا الْمُعَرَّفُ بِلَامِ الْحَقِيقَةِ فَهُوَ مُسَاوٍ فِي الْمَعْنَى لِعَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ ; هَذَا كُلُّهُ حَيْثُ قُلْنَا : إِنَّ عَلَمَ الْجِنْسِ مَعْرِفَةٌ لَفْظًا وَمَعْنًى، أَمَّا إِنْ قُلْنَا : مَعْرِفَةٌ لَفْظًا فَقَطْ فَهُوَ مُرَادِفٌ فِي الْمَعْنَى لِاسْمِ الْجِنْسِ كَمَا يَقُولُهُ ابْنُ مَالِكٍ وَالرَّضِيُّ، فَهُوَ حِينَئِذٍ مِنَ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ، وَإِلَى الْفَرْقِ الْمَذْكُورِ بَيْنَ عَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ وَاسْمِهِ أَشَرْتُ بِقَوْلِي: إِذَا أَرَدْتَ الْفَرْقَ بَيْنَ عَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ وَاسْمِهِ حَقِّقْ وَافْهَم فَالْعَلَمُ الْحَقِيقَةُ الْمُسْتَحْضَرَه فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالْعَ الْفَرْدَ أَنْ تَعْتَبِرَه وَالِاسْمُ لِلْحَقِيقَةِ بِقَيْدِ الْإِفْرَادِ فَذِي طَرِيقَه وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ الِاسْمَ لِلْحَقِيقَةِ مِنْ حَيْثُ مَا هِيَ أَنَّى حَقِيقَة وَالْفَرْدُ أَيْضًا فِيهِ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَر وَأَوَّلُ حَقَّقَهُ ذَوُو النَّظَرِ
His saying: (By consideration of its existence in its external individuals) i.e., so the generic noun is posited for the quiddity alone without its specific individual, and it is called an ambiguous individual, a scattered individual, and a common unity. This is the opinion of al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-Subki, and Sa'd al-Din. And it was said: Indeed the generic noun is posited for the quiddity insofar as it is itself, and its use for the individual is only due to the realization of the quiddity in it and its being a locus for it, and it is a real usage not metaphorical; because the word is used in the reality, and individuality is derived from outside. So the difference between the generic proper noun and its name according to the first opinion is from two aspects: One of them: That the generic proper noun is posited for the quiddity with disregard of its referents, and the generic noun is posited for it with the condition of its existence in one unspecified individual, and it is among the referents. The second: That the generic proper noun indicates by the essence of its word that that reality is known in the mind of the addressee, present in it, conceived for him, while the generic noun does not indicate a covenant at all. As for the second opinion, it is from one aspect: That both of them, even if they are posited for the quiddity with disregard of its individuals, yet in the generic proper noun there is an additional condition, which is the indication by the essence of its word that that reality is known and known to the addressee, while the generic noun does not indicate that except by the tool if it is present. And the disagreement is only concerning the subject of the indefinite generic noun; as for the one defined by the lam of reality, it is equivalent in meaning to the generic proper noun. All this is when we say: The generic proper noun is a definite in wording and meaning. But if we say: It is a definite only in wording, then it is synonymous in meaning with the generic noun, as Ibn Malik and al-Radi say, then it is among the universals. And to the aforementioned difference between the generic proper noun and its name I alluded in my saying: When you want the difference between the generic proper noun and its name, verify and understand: So the proper noun is the reality that is present in the mind, and consider the individual, and the name is for the reality with the condition of individualization, so that is its way. And it was said: Indeed the name is for the reality insofar as it is a reality, and the individual also is not considered in it. And the first was verified by the people of consideration.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion by the commentator clarifying the difference between two types of nouns: 'alam al-jins (generic proper noun) and ism al-jins (generic noun). The discussion is rooted in the first opinion (attributed to al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-Subki, and Sa'd al-Din) that ism al-jins signifies the quiddity with the condition of existence in an unspecified individual, i.e., a singular but not particularised. On the second opinion, ism al-jins signifies the quiddity as such, without any condition of individuality; its application to an individual is a real usage because the quiddity is actually instantiated, not metaphorical. The commentator then delineates the difference between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins according to the first opinion in two respects: (1) 'Alam al-jins disregards referents (it is a proper name for a genus, e.g., 'Asad' for the lion species), whereas ism al-jins (e.g., 'asad' meaning a lion) requires existence in some unspecified individual. (2) 'Alam al-jins inherently indicates that the reality is present and known to the addressee (a conceptual covenant), while ism al-jins does not convey any covenant. According to the second opinion, the difference is only one: both are posited for the quiddity without individuals, but 'alam al-jins adds the condition of indicating a pre-known reality (a mental presence), while ism al-jins does not. The disagreement applies specifically to indefinite generic nouns; the definite generic noun (with the definiteness particle) is equivalent to 'alam al-jins. Finally, if 'alam al-jins is considered definite only in wording, not meaning, then it is synonymous with ism al-jins. The commentator concludes by citing his own poetic summary of the difference. For example, in usul al-fiqh, the generic noun 'khamr' (wine) signifies any intoxicating substance individually, but the generic proper noun 'al-Khamr' (the class of wine) is a known entity in the mind.

علم الجنس

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion, what is a key difference between 'alam al-jins and ism al-jins?
A) 'Alam al-jins is indefinite, ism al-jins is definite.
B) 'Alam al-jins signifies the quiddity with disregard of referents, while ism al-jins requires existence in an unspecified individual.
C) 'Alam al-jins is used only for physical objects, ism al-jins for abstract concepts.
D) Both are identical in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The core distinction is that 'alam al-jins carries a mental covenant and disregards individuals, while ism al-jins either conditions an unspecified individual or does not, depending on the opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'rajul' (man). According to the first opinion, explain whether it is ism al-jins or 'alam al-jins and why.
?
In a fiqh text, the term 'al-ghasb' (usurpation) appears with the definite article. Based on the passage, does this function as ism al-jins or 'alam al-jins? Justify your answer.

What Is Mantiq?

Classifying Book Names: Personal vs Generic Proper Nouns
Debate on classification of book names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'alam shakhs and 'alam jins as applied to book names and explain how the commentator resolves the objection of multiple applications.

Core Idea
A proper noun can name each instance of a book without becoming a generic noun.

This passage explores a dispute among logicians about whether names of books like 'al-Muwatta' are personal proper nouns ('alam shakhs) or generic proper nouns ('alam jins). The commentator explains the reasoning behind the personal proper noun view and addresses a key objection: how can a proper noun apply to multiple distinct copies or recitations? Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyze definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Find the two statements about book names: one considers them 'alam shakhs, the other 'alam jins. Note the key terms.
The dispute is explicitly mentioned at the beginning.
2
Extract the reasoning for 'alam shakhs
For those who say 'alam shakhs, they argue that the name is posited for every specific individual (e.g., each particular copy or recitation) from the accidents and utterances, regardless of source or time.
The passage uses the example of al-Muwatta and compares it to how definite descriptions work.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection is that a personal proper noun cannot apply to multiple distinct individuals, yet book names apply to many copies. The commentator responds by analogizing to a father naming each son 'Muhammad': each son is a distinct individual with the same name. Similarly, the author of a book places the name for each particular instantiation of that specific composition.
The response uses the example of Ibn Malik's 'Tashil al-Fawa'id'.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the name 'al-Quran'. According to the 'alam shakhs view, it is posited for each specific utterance (e.g., from Jibril, from the Prophet, from each reciter) as a distinct individual. Compare with the 'alam jins view that it refers to the kind.
The passage explicitly mentions the Quran and surah names as additional examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدِ اخْتُلِفَ فِي أَسْمَاءِ الكُتُبِ كَـ : «المُوَطَأ»، و«المُدَوَّنَة» مَثَلاً : فَقِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ . وَقِيلَ : مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الْجِنْسِ . فَمَنْ قَالَ: «عَلَمُ شَخْصٍ»، قَالَ : إِنَّ «المُوَطَّأ» مَثَلاً وُضِعَ لِكُلِّ مُعَيَّنٍ بِخُصُوصِهِ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْأَعْرَاضِ وَالْأَلْفَاظِ مِنْ أَيِّ شَخْصٍ صَدَرَتْ، وَفِي أَيِّ زَمَانٍ وَقَعَتْ ; نَظِيرَ مَا مَرَّ فِي وَضْعِ المَعَارِفِ غَيْرِ العَلَمِ. وَقِيلَ: إِنَّ الْفَرْقَ فِي اللَّفْظِ فَقَط مَعْنَاهُمَا مُتَّحِدٌ بِلَا شَطَط وَذَا وَإِنْ قَالَ بِهِ ابْنُ مَالِك وَمَذْهَبُ الرَّضِي عَلَيْهِ سَالِك قَدْ رَدَّهُ الْمُحَقِّقُ المُرَادِي فَانْظُرْهُ كَيْ تَفُوزَ بِالمُرَادِ . قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ: «المُوَطَّأ»، و«المُدَوَّنَة») وَكَذَا أَيْضًا الْقُرْآنُ الْعَظِيمُ وَأَسْمَاءُ سُوَرِهِ كَـ: «الْبَقَرَةُ»، «آلِ عِمْرَانَ»، وَأَسْمَاءُ الْقَصَائِدِ كَـ «الْهَمْزِيَّةُ» وَ «بَانَتْ سُعَادُ». قَوْلُهُ : (فَمَنْ قَالَ عَلَمُ شَخْصٍ قَالَ: إِنَّ «المُوَطَّأ» مَثَلاً ... إِلَخْ) أَشَارَ الشَّارِحُ بِهَذَا الْكَلَامِ إِلَى جَوَابٍ عَنْ سُؤَالٍ أُوْرِدَ عَلَى مَنْ قَالَ : إِنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيلِ عَلَمِ الشَّخْصِ؛ حَاصِلُهُ : أَنَّ العَلَمَ الشَّخْصِيَّ يَسْتَحِيلُ تَعَدُّدُ مَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهِ، وَمَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، فَإِنَّ الْقُرْآنَ كَمَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الصَّادِرِ مِنْ جِبْرِيلَ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ، كَذَلِكَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الصَّادِرِ مِنَ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، وَالصَّادِرُ مِنْ كُلِّ أَحَدٍ بَعْدَهُ، وَاللَّفْظُ عَرَضٌ يَتَعَدَّدُ بِتَعَدُّدِ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ بَلْ وَيَتَعَدَّدُ أَيْضًا بِتَعَدُّدِ الْوَقْتِ وَإِنِ اتَّحَدَ الْمُقَوَّمُ بِهِ، فَإِنَّ اللَّفْظَ الصَّادِرَ مِنْ زَيْدٍ الْآنَ غَيْرُ الصَّادِرِ مِنْهُ قَبْلُ أَوْ بَعْدُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ. وَمُحَصَّلُ الْجَوَابِ مَا عِنْدَ الشَّارِحِ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى. قَوْلُهُ : (نَظِيرَ مَا مَرَّ) مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ كَمَا قَالَ الْهِلَالِيُّ : مَا لَوْ وَضَعَ أَبٌ مَثَلًا لَفْظَ «مُحَمَّدٍ» لِكُلِّ شَخْصٍ مُعَيَّنٍ بِخُصُوصِهِ مِنَ الْأَوْلَادِ الْمَوْلُودِينَ لَهُ بَعْدُ، فَإِنَّ كُلَّ مَنْ وُجِدَ مِنْهُمْ بَعْدُ يَكُونُ «مُحَمَّدٌ» عَلَمًا عَلَيْهِ صه، فَكَذَا يُقَالُ : إِنَّ ابْنَ مَالِكٍ وَضَعَ تَسْهِيلَ الْفَوَائِدِ لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ مُعَيَّنٍ بِخُصُوصِهِ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُؤَلَّفِ بِالتَّأْلِيفِ الْمَخْصُوصِ الَّذِي أَوَّلُهُ : حَامِدًا لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ، وَآخِرُهُ : «وَهَذَا مِمَّا يَنْقَادُ إِلَيْهِ وَلَا يُقَاسُ عَلَيْهِ»، فَكُلُّ فَرْدٍ وُجِدَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِهِ يَكُونُ تَسْهِيلُ الْفَوَائِدِ عَلَمًا عَلَيْهِ؛ سَوَاءٌ صَدَرَ مِنْ مُؤَلِّفِهِ بِخُصُوصِهِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
And it has been disputed concerning the names of books such as 'al-Muwatta' and 'al-Mudawwana' for example: It was said: They are of the category of personal proper noun. And it was said: They are of the category of generic proper noun. So whoever says 'personal proper noun', he says: Indeed 'al-Muwatta' for example is posited for every specific determined individual from those accidents and utterances, from whichever person they originated, and in whichever time they occurred; analogous to what has passed concerning the setting of definite knowledge other than the proper noun. And it was said: The difference is only in wording; their meaning is the same without excess. And this, even though Ibn Malik said it and the school of al-Radi follows it, the verifier al-Muradi refuted it. So examine it so that you may succeed in attaining the intended. His saying: (Like 'al-Muwatta' and 'al-Mudawwana') And similarly also the Great Quran and the names of its surahs like 'al-Baqara', 'Al 'Imran', and the names of odes like 'al-Hamziyya' and 'Banat Su'ad'. His saying: (So whoever says it is a personal proper noun, he says: Indeed 'al-Muwatta' for example... etc.) The commentator indicated by this speech a response to a question raised against those who say they are of the category of personal proper noun; the gist of it: That the personal proper noun is impossible for what it is applied to to be multiple, and what we are dealing with is multiple. For indeed the Quran, just as it is applied to the utterance from Jibril peace be upon him, likewise it is applied to that from the Prophet peace be upon him, and that from everyone after him. And the utterance is an accident that multiplies with the multiplicity of the one who manifests it; indeed it also multiplies with multiplicity of time even if the one manifesting it is the same, for the utterance from Zaid now is other than that from him before or after necessarily. And the summary of the answer is what the commentator (may God have mercy on him) has. His saying: (Analogous to what has passed) Like that as al-Hilali said: What if a father, for example, placed the word 'Muhammad' for every specific determined individual from the children who will be born to him later, then every one of them found later becomes 'Muhammad' as a proper noun for him. So likewise it is said: Ibn Malik placed 'Tashil al-Fawa'id' for every specific determined individual from the individuals of the composite wording with the specific composition which begins with 'Praising God, Lord of the worlds' and ends with 'This is something that yields to it and is not analogized'. So every individual that is found among its individuals becomes 'Tashil al-Fawa'id' as a proper noun for it; whether it emanated from its particular author or from someone else.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of names of books such as 'al-Muwatta' and 'al-Mudawwana'. There are two opinions: some say they are personal proper nouns ('alam shakhs), others say they are generic proper nouns ('alam jins). For those who claim they are personal proper nouns, the commentator explains that such names are posited for each specific particular instance of those accidents (utterances) regardless of who produced them or when. This is analogous to how definite descriptions (ma'arif) other than proper nouns are set. Another view holds that the difference between the two types is merely in wording, not meaning, as claimed by Ibn Malik and al-Radi, but the verifier al-Muradi refuted this. The commentator then expands on examples: book names include the Quran and its surahs, and odes. He addresses a potential objection against the personal proper noun view: a personal proper noun cannot apply to multiple distinct individuals, yet these names apply to multiple instances (e.g., different recitations). The response is that just as a father might name each of his future sons 'Muhammad' and each becomes a distinct proper noun, similarly, the author of 'Tashil al-Fawa'id' intended the name to apply to every particular instantiation of that specific composition, whether from himself or others. Thus, each instantiation is a different individual receiving the same name as a proper noun. For example, the name 'al-Baqara' applies to the specific surah in the Quran, but each copy or recitation of that surah is a different occurrence, yet the name is still a personal proper noun for that specific surah as an ideal. The passage clarifies that linguistic convention allows such multiplicity without violating the nature of proper nouns.

Key Terms
علم الشخص ('alam al-shakhs): A personal proper noun that designates a specific individual, but in this context can be posited for multiple particular instantiations of a work. [Note: In standard logic, 'alam shakhs refers to a proper noun for a particular individual; here it is extended to each occurrence of a text.]
علم الجنس ('alam al-jins): A generic proper noun that signifies the kind itself, as applied to the class of a work. [Note: No significant shift; consistent with earlier lesson.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the name 'al-Muwatta' considered a personal proper noun despite applying to multiple copies?
A) It is a generic proper noun because it refers to a class.
B) It is posited for each specific individual copy as a separate proper noun.
C) It is only a metaphorical usage.
D) It is a common noun.

Key Takeaway
The classification of book names as personal proper nouns is defended by positing the name for each particular instance, analogous to naming multiple children with the same name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Choose a well-known Islamic book name (e.g., 'Sahih al-Bukhari' or 'al-Umm'). According to the two views, explain whether it is 'alam shakhs or 'alam jins, and justify your answer using the reasoning from the passage. Consider the objection of multiplicity: does your view adequately address that the name applies to many copies?

Why Hadith, Mawjud, and Shay' Are Not Genera
Author argues certain broad terms are not genera because extrinsic to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why broad terms like 'existent' are not considered genera in Aristotelian ontology and distinguish essential from accidental predicates in the context of definition.

Core Idea
A genus must be part of the essence; existence is not essential to quiddity.

In previous lessons, we learned about the five universals and that genus (jins) is one of them. Here, the author addresses a crucial refinement: not every broad term qualifies as a genus. He specifically explains why terms like 'the originated,' 'the existent,' and 'the thing' are not genera for the highest genus, substance (jawhar). This distinction hinges on the difference between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) predicates—a cornerstone of rigorous definition in Islamic logic. The passage also includes a refutation of an alternative explanation involving politeness toward the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the candidate terms
The author lists three terms: al-hadith (the originated), al-mawjud (the existent), and al-shay' (the thing). These are more general (a'amm) than jawhar (substance) and thus might be considered genera.
These terms are proposed as possible genera because they apply to more things than jawhar does.
2
Understand the rejection criterion
The author states that these terms are 'outside the essence' (kharij 'an mahiyyatihi) of substance. This means they are not part of the quiddity (mahiyya) of jawhar; they are accidental predicates.
For a predicate to be a genus, it must be essential—part of what the thing is.
3
Classify the terms as accidental
The author clarifies that these terms are 'among the concomitants of existence' (lawazim al-wujud), not of essence. Hence they belong to the category of accidents ('aradiyyat), not essentials (dhatiyyat).
Concomitants of existence are attached to existents but do not define their quiddity.
4
Analyze al-Ghazali's authority
Al-Ghazali explicitly says that existence cannot be an essential because 'the quiddity is conceived without it.' This supports the argument: if you can conceive of a substance without affirming its existence, then existence is not part of its definition.
This is a common epistemological test for essentiality.
5
Refute the politeness objection
Some claimed these terms were omitted out of respect for the Necessary Existent (Allah) because they would apply to Him. The author rejects this: if existence were a genus, it would apply to God, implying composition in His essence—impossible. The omission is logically necessary, not merely polite.
This shows the decision is based on rigorous analysis, not etiquette.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الحَادِثُ وَ: «المَوْجُودُ وَ : «الشَّيْءُ»، فَإِنَّهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الجَوْهَرِ»، لَكِنْ لَيْسَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهَا بِجِنْسِ لَهُ ؛ لِخُرُوجِهَا عَنِ مَاهِيَّتِهِ ، وَقَدْ عَدُّوهَا مِنْ لَوَزَامِ الوُجُودِ لا المَاهِيَّةِ ، فَهِيَ كُلُّهَا مِنَ العَرَضِيَّاتِ، فَلِذَا لَمْ يَذْكُرُوهَا ، وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ الغَزَالِيُّ فِي كُتُبِهِ بِأَنَّ الوُجُودَ لَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنَ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ مِنَ الأَجْنَاسِ العَالِيَةِ قَائِلاً : لِأَنَّ الحَقِيقَةَ تُتَصَوَّرُ بِدُونِهِ؛ أَيْ : فَلِذَا لَمْ يَعُدُّوهُ ، لَا لِمَا قِيْلَ مِنْ أَنَّهُمْ تَرَكُوهُ تَأدُّباً ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَشْمَلُ وَاجِبَ الوُجُودِ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ لَا يُقَالُ إِلَّا لَوْ صَحْ كَوْنُهُ جِنْساً ، وَهُوَ لَا يَصِحُ .
As for 'al-hadith' (the originated), 'al-mawjud' (the existent), and 'al-shay'' (the thing), even though they are more general than 'jawhar' (substance), yet none of them is a genus for it because they are outside its essence. They have counted them among the concomitants of existence, not of essence. Thus they are all among the accidents. For this reason they did not mention them. Al-Ghazali explicitly stated in his books that existence cannot be among the essentials, so it cannot be among the higher genera, saying: 'Because the quiddity is conceived without it.' That is why they did not count it, not because of what is said that they omitted it out of politeness because it includes the Necessary Existent; for that is only said if it were correct for it to be a genus, and it is not correct.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the classification of genera. The author explains why terms like 'hadith' (originated), 'mawjud' (existent), and 'shay'' (thing) are not considered genera for substances like jawhar. Although these terms are more general (a'amm) than jawhar, they are not genera because they do not belong to the essence (mahiyya) of jawhar. Instead, they are among the attendant accidents (lawazim al-wujud) that are extrinsic to essence. The author then cites al-Ghazali's position that existence cannot be an essential (dhati) predicate because the quiddity (haqiqa) of a thing is conceivable without existence. This refutes the possible objection that such terms were omitted out of respect (ta'adub) for the Necessary Existent (Allah). The author argues that this explanation fails because the premise that existence is a genus of the Necessary Existent is false; if it were a genus, it would constitute composition in the Necessary Existent, which is impossible. Thus, the omission is based on logical necessity, not etiquette.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الذاتيات
العرضيات
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the logicians forgot to include it.

Key Takeaway
A term cannot serve as a genus if it is not part of the essence of the species, even if it is more general.

Incomplete Definitions: When a Description Masquerades as a Definition
Author defines and critiques definitions as incomplete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete definition (rasm naqis) by checking whether it lacks a proper genus, using the examples from the passage.

Core Idea
An incomplete definition (rasm naqis) describes without giving the genus — it is a description, not a true definition.

In this passage, the author provides definitions of four categories and then declares them all incomplete. Why? Because they lack a proper genus. Understanding what makes a definition complete or incomplete is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic scholarship, whether in fiqh, kalam, or usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of 'aql, milk, fi'l, and infi'al in the Arabic. Write down each definition in your own words.
The definitions are given in sequence: 'aql, milk, fi'l, infi'al.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
For each definition, identify the broader category (genus) and the distinguishing feature (differentia). For example, milk is defined as 'a state occurring to a body...' – the genus is 'state' (hay'a).
The passage later says there is no conceived genus for these categories because they are highest categories.
3
Apply the test for completeness
Check if the definition includes a true genus that is a higher category. The author says these definitions lack a proper genus because the categories of quality are highest. Therefore, they are incomplete (rasm naqis).
A complete definition (hadd) requires a genus and a differentia.
4
Understand the epistemological consequence
The author notes that the philosophers had no demonstration for the ten categories; they relied on induction (istiqra'). This shows that even the categories are not established with certainty.
Induction does not yield certainty (yaqin) according to the standard Islamic view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ مُثْلَ لَهُ بِمَا يُسَمَّى : «عَقْلاً عِنْدَ الفَلَاسِفَةِ، وَهُوَ مُجَرَّدُ أَيْ : لَيْسَ مُتَحَيِّزاً وَلَا قَائِماً بِمُتَحَيِّز ; بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهُ جِنْسٌ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَأَفْرَادُهُ أَنْوَاعٌ تَحْتَهُ تَخْتَلِفُ بِالفُصُولِ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُهُ نَوْعاً ، وَأَفْرَادُهُ أَشْخَاصٌ تَخْتَلِفُ بِالعَوَارِضِ، وَهَذَا بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ دَاخِلاً عِنْدَهُمْ تَحْتَ جِنْسِ الجَوْهَرِ ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُهُ دَاخِلاَ تَحْتَهُ، فَيَكُونُ نَوْعاً إضَافِيًّا، أَوْ جِنْساً إِضَافِيًّا، وَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ عَلَى هَذَا التَّقْسِيمِ، وَقَدْ غَلَطَ مَنْ حَمَلَهُ عَلَيْهِ ، وَفَسَّرَ القَرِيبَ بِالسَّافِلِ، وَالبَعِيدَ بِالعَالِي، قصارة. بيده الملك، وهو : هيئة تعرض للجسم باعتبار ما يحيط به أو بعضه، وينتقل بانتقاله كالتَّقَمُّص والتَّعَمُّم والتَّخَتُّم، فإن انتقل بانتقاله، ولم يحط كوضع القميص على الرأس، أو أحاط ولم ينتقل كالحال في الخيمة، فليس بملك. «لواه»: الفعل، وهو : تأثيرُ الشَّيء في غير ما دام يؤثر كالمسخن ما دام يسخن . «فالتوى»: الانفعال، وهو : تأثير الشَّيء عن غيره ما دام يتأثر ؛ كـ المسخن ما دام يتسخن، أمَّا السُّخونة والبرودة عقب انتسخين والتبريد فهما غير الانفعال ؛ لبقائهما بعد، ولذلك وقع التقييد بـ «ما دام»، وإنَّما هما من مقولات الكيف، وهذه التعاريف كلُّها رسوم ناقصةٌ؛ إذ لا يُتصوَّر لها جنس كيف وهي العالية، ولم يدَّع الحكماء للحصر في هذه العشرة برهاناً، وإنَّما عمدتهم فيه الاستقراء، وهو مبني على أنَّ الوجود ليس بجنس، كما هو الحق .
It was exemplified by what is called 'aql among the philosophers, and it is abstract; i.e., not extended nor subsisting in an extended thing, based on it being a genus according to them, and its individuals are species under it differing by differentiae, and some make it a species, and its individuals are persons differing by accidents, and this is based on it not being included under the genus of substance according to them, and some make it included under it, so it becomes a relative species or a relative genus, and it is not correct to carry the words of the poet on this division, and whoever carried it upon it made a mistake, and interpreted the near as the lower and the far as the higher. (Marginal note). Possession (milk) is: a state occurring to a body in consideration of what surrounds it or part of it, and it moves with its movement, like putting on a shirt, wearing a turban, and putting on a ring; if it moves with its movement but does not surround, like placing a shirt on the head, or surrounds but does not move, like the situation in a tent, then it is not possession. 'Lawah' (verb) is: the effect of something in another as long as it affects, like a heater as long as it heats. 'Faltawa' (passive) is: the effect of something from another as long as it is affected, like a heater as long as it is heated; as for heat and cold after heating and cooling, they are not the passive because they remain after, therefore the restriction 'as long as' was made; rather they belong to the category of quality. And all these definitions are incomplete descriptions (rusum naqisa) because there is no conceived genus for the category of quality since it is a highest category, and the philosophers did not claim a demonstration for the enumeration of these ten; rather their reliance in it is induction, and it is based on the fact that existence is not a genus, as is the truth.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a commentary providing definitions of several categories: 'aql (intellect), milk (possession), fi'l (action), and infi'al (passion). The author first discusses 'aql as an example, noting that philosophers differ on whether it is a genus or a species under substance. He explains that the correct interpretation of the poet's words is that 'aql is a genus with species differing by differentiae, not a species. Then he defines milk as a relational property of a body being surrounded by something. Then he defines fi'l as an action that inheres as long as the effect lasts, and infi'al as the reception of that effect. He clarifies that heat and cold after heating are not infi'al but qualities. Finally, he critiques these definitions as incomplete (rusum naqisa) because they lack a proper genus; they are descriptions (rusum) not definitions (hudud). He notes that philosophers had no demonstration for the ten categories, relying on induction. This shows the epistemological weakness of the Peripatetic system according to the author.

Classification of Words: Mutawāṭi', Mushakkak, Mushtarak, Mutabāyin
Defines and discusses univocal, analogical, and equivocal words with examples and scholarly critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic term as mutawāṭi', mushakkak, mushtarak, or mutabāyin by applying the criteria of equality, accidental difference, and essential difference of meaning across individuals.

Core Idea
Word classification hinges on whether core meaning is equal, accidentally varied, or essentially distinct.

Words relate to meanings in three key ways: some apply equally to all individuals, some vary by accident, and some have multiple distinct cores. This lesson unpacks the logical categories of mutawāṭi', mushakkak, mushtarak, and mutabāyin as defined by classical logicians, with crucial refinements from al-Yūsī and Ibn Marzūq that resolve doubts about borderline cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its individuals
Pick a term (e.g., 'sun', 'human', 'existence'). List the individuals or instances it refers to. Ask: does the core meaning appear the same across all instances?
For abstract terms, consider mental as well as actual individuals.
2
Test for equality of core meaning
If all individuals share the core meaning equally without any perceived priority or difference, the term is mutawāṭi' (univocal). Example: 'sun' (individual suns are equal in the concept of sun). If not, proceed.
Ignore accidental differences like brightness or size; focus on the essence.
3
Check for accidental differences
If individuals differ in intensity, priority, or strength of the meaning, but the core concept remains the same, it is mushakkak (analogical). Example: 'light' in lamp vs. sun. If the difference is not accidental but essential, it is mushtarak.
The difference must be such that one doubts whether it is essential or accidental.
4
Determine essential difference or equivalence
If individuals have completely different meanings with no common core, it is mushtarak (equivocal) if a single word, or mutabāyin (distinct) if multiple words. Example: 'existence' for God vs. creation is mushtarak per the verified view.
Mutabāyin is a single word with multiple unrelated meanings (e.g., 'man' and 'horse' as homonyms).
5
Apply the epistemic criterion of Ibn Marzūq
If you are certain the word was coined for the common core only, it is mutawāṭi'. If certain it was coined with particularities, it is mushtarak. If uncertain, it is mushakkak. This resolves difficult cases.
This pragmatic definition guards against overthinking historical coinage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
أَوِ الذِّهْنِيَّةُ كَـ: «الشَّمْسِ»، فَإِنَّ أَفْرَادَهُ المُقَدَّرَةُ وَالفَرْدُ المَوْجُودُ مِنْهُ كُلُّهَا مُتَسَاوِيَةٌ فِي مَعْنَى الشَّمْسِ لَا يُدْرِكُ العَقْلُ رُجَحَاناً لِبَعْضٍ مِنْهَا عَلَى بَعْضٍ.
وَسُمِّيَ هَذَا : «مُتَوَاطِناً»؛ لأَنَّ التَّوَاطُوَ لُغَةٌ : «التَّوَافُقُ»، وَأَفْرَادُهُ مُتَوَافِقَةٌ فِيهِ لَمْ تَخْتَلِف .
وَالمُشَكَّكُ هُوَ : «الكُلّيُّ الَّذِي اخْتَلَفَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ فِيهِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ وُجُودُهُ فِي بَعْضِهَا أَكْثَرَ كَـ: «البَيَاضِ»، فَإِنَّهُ فِي التَّلْجِ أَكْثَرُ مِنْهُ فِي العَاجِ، أَوْ يَكُونَ أَقْدَمَ أَوْ أَقْوَى كَـ: «الوُجُودِ»
وَجْهُ الدَّلِيلِ: أَنَّ لِبَعْضِ أَفْرَادِهِ تَقَدُّماً عَلَى البَعْضِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ مُتَوَاطِئاً.
فالجواب : أنَّ الاختلاف بتلك الأوصاف ليس راجعاً إلى نفس الحقيقة، بل هو من عوارض وجودها في ضمن أفرادها، وليس المراد الأولوية أو الأقدمية أو الأشديَّة في الوجود، بل في الاتصاف بمفهوم اللفظ ; بمعنى : أنَّ العقل إذا حاول مطابقة المفهوم لكثيرين وجد بعض الأفراد أولى بهذا المفهوم، أو أقدم، أو أشدُّ ، وأفراد الإنسان ليست كذلك؛ لأنَّ مطابقة الإنسانية لجميعها على السَّويَّة والتَّقدُّم إنَّما هو في وجودها ، فافهم، نقله شيخنا سيدي الطيب. قوله : (بِأَنْ يَكُونَ وُجُودُهُ فِي بَعْضِهَا أَكْثَرَ معناه : أن يكون لفظ موضوع لمعنى، وذلك المعنى موجود في أفراده، وهو مختلف فيها بأن يكون وجوده في بعضها أكثر من وجوده في الآخر، أو أشدَّ كـ «النور»، فإنَّ لفظه موضوع لمعنى وهو الشُّعاع، وذلك المعنى موجود في السراج والشَّمس، لكن وجوده في الشَّمس أكثر ، ونظيرُهُ: «البياض» الذي في مثال الشارح.
قوله : (أَوْ أَقْوَى) تبع فيه الهلاليّ والصَّوابُ : « أو أولى؛ إذ الأقوى هو الأشدُّ والأكثر . قوله: (كـ: «الوُجُودِ) أي : فإنَّه موجود في واجب الوجود، وفي الممكن الوجود، لكنَّه في واجب الوجود قديم، وفي الممكن حادث، وقد اجتمع في لفظ «الوجود» ثلاثة أمور كلُّها يحصل بها التشكيك، فإنَّه أقوى؛ لكون آثاره أقوى من آثار الممكنات، وأقدم، وأولى. اهـ. قال الحفيد في «شرح التهذيب ما نصه : وهنا بحث، وهو أنَّهم جعلوا الأشدية باعتبار كثرة الآثار أو كمالها، والظاهرُ : أنَّ ذلك يوجد في المتواطئ كـ «الإنسان»؛ إذ بعض أفراده كنبينا محمد لا أكثر وأكمل بحسب الخواص الإنسانيَّة كالإدراك وغيره. اهـ.
فَإِنَّهُ فِي القَدِيمِ أَقْوَى مِنْهُ فِي الحَادِثِ وَأَسْبَقُ ، وَهَذَا عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الوُجُودِ فِي القَدِيمِ وَالحَادِثِ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا فِي الحَقِيقَةِ، وَأَنَّ لَفْظَ «الوُجُودِ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، لَا المُشَكَّكِ.
الاسم: الشَّكُ، مَا تَفَاوَتَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ: «مُشَكَّكاً» ; لِأَنَّهُ يُشَكَّكُ النَّاظِرُ فِيهِ; أَيْ : يُوقِعُهُ في الشَّكِّ، فَلَا يَدْرِي هَلْ أَفْرَادُهُ مُتَّحِدَةٌ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ فَيَكُونُ مُتَوَاطِناً ، أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَةٌ فِيهَا فَيَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكاً ، كَذَا قَالُوا ; وَالتَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخ اليُوسِيِّ : أَنَّ المُشَكَّكَ لَا تَفَاوُتَ بَيْنَ أَفْرَادِهِ فِي أَصْلِ الحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِنَّمَا تَفَاوُتُ بِالعَوَارِضِ، فَعَلَى هَذَا يَكُونُ المُشَكَّكُ هُوَ مَا تَفَاوَتَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ تَفَاوُتاً بَيِّناً يُتَوَهَّمُ رُجُوعُهُ لِلْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ عِنْدَ التَّحْقِيقِ رَاجِعاً لِلْعَوَارِضِ .
القِسْمُ الثَّانِي مِنَ الأَرْبَعَةِ : مُقَابِلُ الأَوَّلِ ; أَيْ : لَفْظُ مُتَعَدِّدُ لِمَعْنَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ، وَهُوَ : المُتباينُ : كَـ: رَجُلٍ، وَ: «فَرَسٍ، وَ : كِتَابٍ)).
والجواب عنه ما تقدَّم نقله عن شيخنا سيديِّ الطَّيِّب . قوله : (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا ... إلخ) أي: حقيقة وجود القديم مباينة لحقيقة وجود الحادث; لأنَّ تباين اللوازم ملزوم لتباين الملزومات، فيكون إذاً لفظ الوجود من قبيل المشترك. قوله: (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخِ المُوسِيِّ : أَنَّ المُشَكَّكَ لَا تَفَاوُتَ... إلخ) أي: لأنَّ هذا التفاوت إن كان داخلاً في مفهوم اللفظ كان مشتركاً، وإن كان خارجاً عنه كان أصل المعنى حاصلاً في الكلِّ على السواء; إذ لا عبرة بالخارج، فيكون في الحقيقة من قبيل المتواطئ، وعلى كلٍّ أن يعد قسماً برأسه ولكن لما كان هذا التّفاوتُ بالعوارض تفاوتاً بيناً يتوهم رجوعه للحقيقة، وإن كان عند التحقيق راجعاً للعوارض عدوه قسماً على حِدَته مقابلاً لما ليس فيه هذا التفاوت، هذا ما أشار إليه اليوسي.
وقال ابن مرزوق: ما تحققنا فيه الوضع للقدر المشترك مع عدم اعتبار ما وقع به التفاوت سميناه: «متواطئاً»، وما تحقَّقنا الوضع له مع الخصوصيَّات سميناه : مشتركاً»، وما شككنا فيه سميناه بـ «المشكك»، فتكون حقيقة المشكك اصطلاحاً هو اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى مختلف في محاله لم يدر قصد الواضع منه هل هو القدر المشترك فقط، أو هو مع الخصوصيَّات، والله تعالى أعلم.
Chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. Or the mental [sun], like 'the sun', for its estimated individuals and the existing individual from it are all equal in the meaning of 'sun'; the intellect does not perceive a preponderance of some over others. And this is called 'mutawāṭi’' (univocal) because muwāṭa’a linguistically is 'agreement', and its individuals agree in it, not differing. And the mushtakk (analogical) is the universal whose individuals differ in it, such that its existence in some of them is more, like 'whiteness', for it is in snow more than in ivory; or it may be older or stronger, like 'existence'. The evidence: some of its individuals have precedence over others, so it is not univocal. The answer: The difference by those attributes does not pertain to the essence itself, but is from the accidents of its existence within the individuals; and what is intended is not priority, seniority, or intensity in existence, but in the qualification with the concept of the word. Meaning: when the intellect attempts to match the concept to many, it finds some individuals more worthy of this concept, or older, or stronger, and the individuals of 'human' are not like that because the matching of humanity to all of them is equal; progress is only in their existence. Understand. Transmitted by our teacher Sayyidī al-Ṭayyib. His saying: 'such that its existence in some is more' means: that a word is placed for a meaning, and that meaning exists in its individuals, and it differs in them such that its existence in some is more than its existence in others, or stronger, like 'light', for its word is placed for a meaning which is radiance, and that meaning exists in the lamp and the sun, but its existence in the sun is more; and its parallel is 'whiteness' in the example of the commentator. His saying: 'or stronger' – in this he followed al-Hilālī and the correct [view] is 'or more worthy', for 'stronger' is the more intense and more. His saying: 'like existence' – i.e., it exists in the Necessary Existent and in the possible existent, but in the Necessary Existent it is eternal and in the possible it is originated; and in the word 'existence' three matters are combined, all of which produce ambiguity (tashkīk); for it is stronger because its effects are stronger than the effects of possibles, and older, and more worthy. End. Al-Ḥafīd said in his commentary on al-Tahdhīb, verbatim: 'There is a discussion here, namely that they made intensity depend on abundance or perfection of effects, and apparently that occurs in univocal [terms] like 'human'; for some of its individuals, like our Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, are more and more perfect in terms of human properties, such as perception and others.' End. For it is in the Eternal stronger than in the originated and older; and this is on the view that the reality of existence in the Eternal and the originated is one; but the verification is that they are mutually distinct in reality, and that the word 'existence' is of the type of mushtarak (equivocal), not mushakkak. The name: 'the doubt, whose individuals differ' [is called] 'mushakkak' because it makes the observer doubt; i.e., it causes him doubt, so he does not know whether its individuals are united in reality so it would be univocal, or different in it so it would be equivocal. Thus they said. And the verification according to al-Shaykh al-Yūsī is that the mushakkak has no difference between its individuals in the essence of the reality; rather, they differ by accidents. So on this, the mushakkak is that whose individuals differ in a clear difference that is imagined to return to the reality, although upon investigation it returns to accidents. The second division of the four: opposite of the first; i.e., a single word for multiple meanings, and it is the mutabāyin (distinct), like: 'man', 'horse', 'book'. And the answer to it is what was previously transmitted from our teacher Sayyidī al-Ṭayyib. His saying: 'and the verification is that they are distinct... etc.' i.e., the reality of the Eternal's existence is distinct from the reality of the originated's existence; because the distinctness of necessary concomitants entails the distinctness of the things entailed; then the word 'existence' accordingly is of the type of mushtarak. His saying: 'and the verification as with al-Shaykh al-Mūsī: that the mushakkak has no difference... etc.' i.e., because this difference, if it entered the concept of the word, it would be mushtarak; if it were outside it, the original meaning would be obtained in all equally, since no heed is paid to what is external; so in reality it would be of the type of mutawāṭi’. Nevertheless it should be counted a distinct division, but because this difference is by accidents, a clear difference that is imagined to return to the reality, even if upon investigation it returns to accidents, they counted it as a separate division opposite to that which does not have this difference. This is what al-Yūsī indicated. And Ibn Marzūq said: 'What we are certain of – the placement for the common core without considering the difference that occurs – we call it mutawāṭi’; and what we are certain of – the placement for it along with the particularities – we call it mushtarak; and what we doubt about – we call it mushakkak. So the reality of mushakkak terminologically is the word placed for a meaning that differs in its instances, not knowing the intent of the coiner – whether it is only the common core or is it with the particularities. And God Almighty knows best.'
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical discussion of the classification of words (alfāẓ) in relation to their meanings, a foundational topic in logic (al-Manṭiq) and legal theory (Uṣūl al-Fiqh). The author begins by defining the univocal term (mutawāṭi’), exemplified by 'sun' – all individuals, both actual and hypothetical, equally share the meaning without any perceived priority. It is called 'mutawāṭi’’ because the individuals 'agree' (tawāṭu’) in that meaning. Next, the analogical or ambiguous term (mushakkak) is defined as a universal whose individuals differ in the meaning, such that existence (wu jūd) is stronger, older, or more intense in some than others; e.g., whiteness is more intense in snow than in ivory. The author then notes an objection: if some individuals have precedence, then the term is not univocal. The response clarifies that the difference is not in the essence of the concept but in accidental attributes of its existence; the intended priority is in the qualification (ittiṣāf) with the concept, not in the external existence. For example, 'light' is more perfectly manifested in the sun than in a lamp, but the core meaning (radiance) is the same. The author then critiques the example of 'existence' used in the original text, noting that on one view existence is univocal, but the verified position is that the existence of the Necessary Existent is distinct from that of the possible, making 'existence' an equivocal term (mushtarak). A further discussion from al-Ḥafīd raises the point that even in univocal terms like 'human', individuals differ in perfection, so the strong intensity might be found even in univocals. The passage then turns to the etymological meaning of 'mushakkak': it causes doubt (shakk) whether individuals share one reality (univocal) or different realities (equivocal). The author presents al-Yūsī’s verification: in mushakkak, the difference is only accidental, not essential, but it is so stark that one might imagine it essential, thus it is treated as a separate category. The second division is the distinct term (mutabāyin), where a single word has multiple completely different meanings (e.g., 'man', 'horse', 'book'). The passage concludes with Ibn Marzūq’s pragmatic definition: if we are certain that the word was placed for the common core alone, it is mutawāṭi’; if for the particularities, it is mushtarak; if we doubt, it is mushakkak. This nuanced approach guards against oversimplifying the classification by recognizing the epistemic uncertainty in determining the coiner’s intent.

مُشَكَّك
مُشْتَرَك
مُتَبَايِن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the verified view of al-Yūsī regarding the term 'mushakkak'?
A) Its individuals differ in the essence of the reality.
B) Its individuals differ in a clear way that is imagined to return to reality but actually returns to accidents.
C) It is synonymous with mushtarak.
D) It only applies to words with physical intensity like light.

Key Takeaway
The classification of words into mutawāṭi', mushakkak, mushtarak, and mutabāyin depends on whether the core meaning is equal, accidentally varying, or essentially distinct, with doubt about the coiner's intent defining mushakkak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the term 'nur' (light) as used for the sun and a lamp. Is it mutawāṭi', mushakkak, or mushtarak? Justify using the criteria from the passage.
?
Ibn Marzūq states that if we are uncertain about the coiner's intent, the term is mushakkak. Apply this to the term 'qadr' (power/decree) in Qur'an: is it likely mushtarak or mushakkak? Explain your reasoning.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
The Definition and Nature of Mushakkak
Author defines mushakkak and discusses its nature with scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mushakkak, distinguish it from mutawatii and mushtarak, and summarize al-Yusi's and Ibn Marzuq's positions on its nature.

Core Idea
Mushakkak is a term caught between univocal and homonymous, defined by doubt.

The passage opens by discussing the nature of existence in the eternal versus temporal, asserting that 'existence' is mushtarak, not mushakkak. It then defines mushakkak as a term whose individuals differ such that the examiner doubts whether they share the same reality (making it univocal) or differ (making it homonymous). The commentator presents al-Yusi's refinement that mushakkak individuals differ only in accidents, not essence, so it is essentially univocal but treated as a separate category. Ibn Marzuq defines it based on uncertainty about the intent of the word's placement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root meaning of mushakkak
Mushakkak literally means 'causing doubt' or 'equivocal'. The passage states it is so named because it makes the examiner doubtful, not knowing if its individuals are united in reality (mutawatii) or different (mushtarak).
2
Understand the two possibilities for individuals
The examiner considers two possibilities: either the individuals share a common reality (univocal, mutawatii) or they have different specific meanings (homonymous, mushtarak). Mushakkak is the term when this is unknown.
This is the initial definition given by 'they said'.
3
Analyze al-Yusi's refinement
Al-Yusi argues that in mushakkak, the difference between individuals is only in accidents (ʻawāriḍ), not in the root reality. Therefore, it is truly univocal in essence, but because the accidental difference is manifest, one might imagine it returns to reality. Scholars treat it as a separate category.
According to al-Yusi, mushakkak is essentially mutawatii.
4
Examine Ibn Marzuq's definition
Ibn Marzuq defines mushakkak terminologically as a word placed for a meaning that differs in its instances, where the intention of the placers is unknown—whether they intended the common factor only or the common factor with specificities.
This highlights the uncertainty element central to mushakkak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخ البناني على السلم المرونق فَإِنَّهُ فِي القَدِيمِ أَقْوَى مِنْهُ فِي الحَادِثِ وَأَسْبَقُ ، وَهَذَا عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الوُجُودِ فِي القَدِيمِ وَالحَادِثِ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا فِي الحَقِيقَةِ، وَأَنَّ لَفْظَ «الوُجُودِ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، لَا المُشَكَّكِ. الشَّكُ، مَا تَفَاوَتَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ: «مُشَكَّكاً» ; لِأَنَّهُ يُشَكَّكُ النَّاظِرُ فِيهِ؛ أَيْ : يُوقِعُهُ وسمي في (۲) فَلَا يَدْرِ هَلْ أَفْرَادُهُ مُتَّحِدَةٌ (۱) فِي الحَقِيقَةِ فَيَكُونُ مُتَوَاطِناً ، أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَةٌ فِيهَا فَيَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكاً (٢) ، كَذَا قَالُوا ; وَالتَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخ اليُوسِيِّ : أَنَّ المُشَكَّكَ لَا تَفَاوتَ بَيْنَ أَفْرَادِهِ فِي أَصْلِ الحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِنَّمَا تَفَاتَتْ بِالعَوَارِضِ، فَعَلَى هَذَا يَكُونُ المُشَكَّكُ هُوَ مَا تَفَاوِتَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ تَفَاوتاً بَيِّناً يُتَوَهَّمُ رُجُوعُهُ لِلْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ عِنْدَ التَّحْقِيقِ رَاجِعاً [أ/۲۳] لِلْعَوَارِضِ . القِسْمُ الثَّانِي مِنَ الأَرْبَعَةِ : مُقَابِلُ الأَوَّلِ ; أَيْ : لَفْظُ مُتَعَدِّدُ لِمَعْنَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ، وَهُوَ : المُتباينُ :گـ رَجُلٍ، وَ: «فَرَسٍ، وَ : كِتَابٍ)) . وَ: قصارة . والجواب عنه ما تقدَّم نقله عن شيخنا سيديِّ الطَّيِّب . قوله : (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا ... إلخ) أي: حقيقة وجود القديم مباينة لحقيقة وجود الحادث; لأنَّ تباين اللوازم ملزوم لتباين الملزومات، فيكون إذاً لفظ الوجود من قبيل المشترك. قوله: (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخِ المُوسِيِّ : أَنَّ المُشَكَّكَ لَا تَفَاوتَ... إلخ) أي: لأنَّ هذا التفاوت إن كان داخلاً في مفهوم اللفظ كان مشتركاً، وإن كان خارجاً عنه كان أصل المعنى حاصلاً في الكلِّ على السواء؛ إذ لا عبرة بالخارج، فيكون في الحقيقة من قبيل المتواطئ وعلى كل أن يعد قسماً برأسه ولكن لما كان هذا التّفاوتُ بالعوارض تفاوتاً بيناً يتوهم رجوعه للحقيقة، وإن كان عند التحقيق راجعاً للعوارض عدوه قسماً على حدته مقابلاً لما ليس فيه هذا التفاوت، هذا ما أشار إليه اليوسي. فلا ينبغي وقال ابن مرزوق: ما تحققنا فيه الوضع للقدر المشترك مع عدم اعتبار ما وقع به التفاوت سميناه: «متواطئاً»، وما تحقَّقنا الوضع له مع الخصوصيَّات سميناه : مشتركاً»، وما شككنا فيه سميناه بـ «المشكك»، فتكون حقيقة المشكك اصطلاحاً هو اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى مختلف في محاله لم يدر قصد الواضع منه هل هو القدر المشترك فقط، أو هو مع الخصوصيَّات، والله تعالى أعلم.
Commentary of al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-Murawniq: For it is in the eternal stronger than in the temporal and prior. This is according to the view that the reality of existence in the eternal and temporal is one, but the verified position is that they differ in reality, and that the word 'existence' is of the type of mushtarak (homonym), not mushakkak (equivocal). The doubtful [term] is that whose individuals differ: it is called 'mushakkak' because it makes the examiner doubtful; i.e., it places him in doubt, so he does not know whether its individuals are united in reality, thus being mutawatii (univocal), or different in it, thus being mushtarak. Thus they said. The verified position, as with Shaykh al-Yusi, is that mushakkak has no difference between its individuals in the root of reality, but rather they differ in accidents. According to this, mushakkak is that whose individuals differ in a manifest difference that makes one imagine it returns to reality, even if in verification it returns to accidents. The second of the four [categories]: opposite of the first, i.e., a single word for multiple meanings, and it is mutabayin (different), like 'man', 'horse', and 'book', and [unclear]. The answer to it is what was previously transmitted from our master Sayyid al-Tayyib. His saying: 'and the verification is that they differ...' means: the reality of existence of the eternal is different from the reality of existence of the temporal, because the difference of concomitants necessitates the difference of the things they are concomitant to, thus the word 'existence' is of the type of mushtarak. His saying: 'and the verification, as with Shaykh al-Yusi, is that mushakkak has no difference...etc.' means: because this difference, if it is included in the concept of the word, it is mushtarak, and if it is outside it, then the root meaning is realized in all equally, since there is no consideration of what is external, so in reality it is of the type of mutawatii, and in any case it is counted as a separate division, but because this difference in accidents is a manifest difference that one imagines returns to reality, even if in verification it returns to accidents, they counted it as a separate division opposite to that which does not have this difference. This is what al-Yusi indicated. So it is not appropriate. And Ibn Marzuq said: 'What we verified regarding placement for the common factor without considering what caused the difference, we call it mutawatii; what we verified placement for with specificities, we call it mushtarak; and what we doubted in, we call it mushakkak.' So the reality of mushakkak terminologically is the word placed for a meaning that differs in its instances, and the intention of the placers is not known whether it is the common factor only or with specificities. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a section about the classification of terms. It opens with a discussion on the strength of existence in the eternal versus the temporal, asserting that existence is a mushtarak (homonym) rather than mushakkak (equivocal). Then it defines mushakkak: it is a term whose individuals differ such that the examiner is uncertain whether they share the same reality (making it univocal) or differ (making it homonymous). The commentator presents al-Yusi's view that mushakkak individuals do not differ in essence but only in accidents, so the term is technically univocal in root meaning but appears equivocal due to manifest accidental differences. The passage then introduces the second of four categories of words—mutabayin (different)—where a single word has multiple distinct meanings. It includes an example and a cross-reference to a prior answer. Following that, the commentator returns to the mushakkak discussion, explaining that if the difference is within the word's concept it is homonymous; if outside, the root meaning is the same for all, thus it is univocal, but due to the manifest accidental difference, scholars treat it as a separate category. Finally, Ibn Marzuq's definition summarizes: mutawatii is for the common factor, mushtarak for specificities, and mushakkak for cases of doubt. This lesson highlights the subtlety of mushakkak as a disputed term bordering between univocal and homonymous.

Key Takeaway
Mushakkak is a term that causes doubt about whether its meaning is shared or multiple, and scholars like al-Yusi and Ibn Marzuq offer refinements to clarify its nature.

What Is Mantiq?

The Four Categories of Words in Relation to Meaning
Author introduces the second of four word categories: mutabayin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four categories of words (mutawatii, mushtarak, mushakkak, mutabayin) and describe how they are distinguished based on the relationship between word and meaning.

Core Idea
Words are classified by whether they have one meaning, multiple distinct meanings, or cause doubt about their meaning.

In the passage, after discussing mushakkak, the commentator introduces the second of four categories: mutabayin. The four categories are: mutawatii (univocal, one meaning for all individuals), mushtarak (homonym, one word for multiple specific meanings), mushakkak (equivocal, causes doubt), and mutabayin (different words, each with a distinct meaning). This classification is fundamental in Islamic logic for analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
The passage alludes to a fourfold division. The first category is mutawatii (univocal), where a single word has one meaning that applies equally to all its individuals. The second is mushtarak (homonym), where a single word has multiple distinct meanings. The third is mushakkak (equivocal), where the word causes doubt about whether its meaning is one or multiple. The fourth is mutabayin (different), where distinct words each have their own distinct meaning.
The passage explicitly names mushtarak, mushakkak, and mutabayin, and implies mutawatii as the opposite of mutabayin.
2
Contrast mutawatii and mutabayin
Mutawatii is a single word for a single meaning applied equally (e.g., 'insan' for all humans). Mutabayin is multiple words each with a distinct meaning (e.g., 'rajul', 'faras', 'kitab'). They are opposites: one word vs. many words for many meanings.
Mutabayin is the second category mentioned in the passage.
3
Distinguish mushtarak from mushakkak
Mushtarak is known to have multiple specific meanings (e.g., 'ayn for eye, spring). Mushakkak is uncertain: one does not know if the meaning is common or specific. The passage uses the example of 'existence' as mushtarak, not mushakkak.
The difference lies in certainty: mushtarak is certain multiplicity; mushakkak is doubt.
4
Apply to Islamic terms
In Islamic sciences, many words are classified into these categories. For example, 'salat' is mutawatii for the ritual prayer, 'quru' is mushtarak (menstrual period or purity), and 'wajib' might be mushakkak when debated among scholars.
This classification is used in usul al-fiqh to determine how to interpret texts.

شخ البناني على السلم المرونق فَإِنَّهُ فِي القَدِيمِ أَقْوَى مِنْهُ فِي الحَادِثِ وَأَسْبَقُ ، وَهَذَا عَلَى القَوْلِ بِأَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الوُجُودِ فِي القَدِيمِ وَالحَادِثِ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا فِي الحَقِيقَةِ، وَأَنَّ لَفْظَ «الوُجُودِ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، لَا المُشَكَّكِ. الشَّكُ، مَا تَفَاوَتَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ: «مُشَكَّكاً» ; أَيْ : لَفْظُ مُتَعَدِّدُ لِمَعْنَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ، وَهُوَ : المُتباينُ :گـ رَجُلٍ، وَ: «فَرَسٍ، وَ : كِتَابٍ)) . وَ: قصارة . والجواب عنه ما تقدَّم نقله عن شيخنا سيديِّ الطَّيِّب . قوله : (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ تَبَايِنُهُمَا ... إلخ) أي: حقيقة وجود القديم مباينة لحقيقة وجود الحادث; لأنَّ تباين اللوازم ملزوم لتباين الملزومات، فيكون إذاً لفظ الوجود من قبيل المشترك. قوله: (وَالتَّحْقِيقُ كَمَا عِنْدَ الشَّيْخِ المُوسِيِّ : أَنَّ المُشَكَّكَ لَا تَفَاوتَ... إلخ) أي: لأنَّ هذا التفاوت إن كان داخلاً في مفهوم اللفظ كان مشتركاً، وإن كان خارجاً عنه كان أصل المعنى حاصلاً في الكلِّ على السواء؛ إذ لا عبرة بالخارج، فيكون في الحقيقة من قبيل المتواطئ وعلى كل أن يعد قسماً برأسه ولكن لما كان هذا التّفاوتُ بالعوارض تفاوتاً بيناً يتوهم رجوعه للحقيقة، وإن كان عند التحقيق راجعاً للعوارض عدوه قسماً على حدته مقابلاً لما ليس فيه هذا التفاوت، هذا ما أشار إليه اليوسي. فلا ينبغي وقال ابن مرزوق: ما تحققنا فيه الوضع للقدر المشترك مع عدم اعتبار ما وقع به التفاوت سميناه: «متواطئاً»، وما تحقَّقنا الوضع له مع الخصوصيَّات سميناه : مشتركاً»، وما شككنا فيه سميناه بـ «المشكك»، فتكون حقيقة المشكك اصطلاحاً هو اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى مختلف في محاله لم يدر قصد الواضع منه هل هو القدر المشترك فقط، أو هو مع الخصوصيَّات، والله تعالى أعلم.

What Is Mantiq?

Responding to Objections about the Exhaustiveness of the Word Division
Author responds to two objections about the exhaustiveness of the word meaning division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why contradiction is included in complete difference and why synonyms are excluded from the division of different concepts.

Core Idea
Contradiction is a type of complete difference; synonyms are one concept, so they don't count.

The author faces two objections: first, that the division of word meanings is not exhaustive because it lacks contradiction; second, that it wrongly includes synonyms. By answering these, he clarifies the true scope of the classification. This lesson walks through these objections and how the author resolves them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The objector claims the division omits contradiction (tanāquḍ). Why might someone think that?
Contradiction seems like a separate relation, not just difference.
2
Find the author's reply
He classifies contradiction under complete difference (tabāyun kullī). Why are contradictories like 'animal' and 'not animal' completely different?
Because they never coincide in any instance.
3
Identify the second objection
Synonyms like 'insān' and 'bashar' would be included in the second division (they do not differ at all). Why is that a problem?
Synonymy is not a relation of difference, so it shouldn't be a division of difference.
4
Understand the author's response
The division concerns two different concepts (ma‘qūlayn mukhtalifayn). Synonyms share one concept, so they fall outside the division.
The subject of the division is 'two concepts that are different'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
فَإِنْ قِيْلَ : القِسْمَةُ غَيْرُ حَاصِرَةٍ؛ لِبَقَاءِ التَّنَاقُضِ.
قُلْنَا : هُوَ دَاخِلٌّ فِي القِسْم الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ التَّبَايِنُ الكُلّيُّ؛ لأنَّ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ البَتَّةَ كـ: حَيَوَانٍ، وَ لَا حَيَوَانِ، وَمِثْلُهُ التَّضَادُّ فِي نَحْوِ : «البَيَاضِ، وَ: السَّوَادِ»، وَالتَّضَايفُ فِي مِثْلِ: «الأبُوَّةِ، وَ البُنُوَّةِ».
فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: قَوْلُكَ فِي القِسْم الثَّانِي: أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَيَّةَ يَشْمَلُ المُتَرَادِفَيْنِ كـ: الإِنْسَانِ، وَ : البَشَر»، وَلَا يَصِحُ عَلَى هَذَا (۱): عَدُّ التَّرَادُفِ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ التَّبَاينِ . قُلْتُ : جَعْلُ مَوْضُوعِ القِسْمَةِ المَعْقُولَيْنِ ؛ أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ المُخْتَلِفَيْنِ يَمْنَعُ شُمُول المُتَرَادِفَيْن لاتِّحَادِ مَفْهُومِهِما .
وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فكذلك نقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التَّباين :
١ - فالأوَّل ؛ نحو: الإنسان، والأبيض فبينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه وهو ظاهر، ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان ولا أبيض وهما كذلك ؛ لاجتماعهما في الغراب مثلاً، وانفراد الأوّل في الثلج، والثاني في الزنجي.
٢ - والثاني كـ «الحَيَوان، ولا إنسان بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه؛ لاجتماعهما في الفرس مثلاً، وانفراد الحيوان في الإنسان، ولا إنسان في الجماد؛ ونقيضاهما: «لا حيوان، وإنسان وهما متباينان ضرورةً .
وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق فيلزم أن يكون بين نقيضيهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق كذلك، لكن على العكس؛ أي: نقيض الأعمّ أخصُّ، ونقيضُ الأخص أعم، وذلك ك: «الإنسان والحيوان»، فالإنسان أخصُّ، ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، ولا حيوان»؛ فـ «لا إنسان» أعم من لا حيوان»؛ لصدق لا إنسان على ما صدق عليه لا حيوان» من كل مباين للحيوان، وزيادته عليه بصدقه على غير الإنسان من أنواع الحيوان، وذلك واضح، وقد أشار ابن مرزوق إلى النسب الأربع التي بين النقائض بقوله :
فَذُو المسَاوِي وَالعُمُومُ المُطلَق نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مثله فَحَقِّق
(۱) على هذا زيادة من الطبعة الأميرية فقط .
Chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. If it is said: 'The division is not exhaustive because contradiction remains.' We say: 'It is included in the first division, which is complete difference, because two contradictories never come together at all, e.g., animal and not animal. And similarly, opposition like whiteness and blackness, and correlation like fatherhood and sonhood.' If you say: 'Your statement in the second division: that they do not differ at all includes synonyms, like human and man, and it is not valid upon this to count synonymy from the divisions of difference.' I say: 'Making the subject of the division the two concepts, i.e., the two different meanings, prevents the inclusion of synonyms due to the unity of their concept.' As for those two which have generality and particularity from one aspect, likewise their two opposites never have between them except generality and particularity from one aspect or difference. First: e.g., human and white; between them is generality and particularity from one aspect, and that is apparent. Their two opposites: (not human and not white) are likewise, because they combine in e.g., a crow, and the first is unique in snow, and the second in a black person. Second: e.g., animal and not human; between them is generality and particularity from one aspect, because they combine in e.g., a horse, and animal is unique in human, and not human in inorganic things; and their two opposites: not animal and human are necessarily different. As for those two which have absolute generality and particularity, it follows that between their two opposites there is absolute generality and particularity likewise, but reversed; i.e., the opposite of the more general is more specific, and the opposite of the more specific is more general. That is like human and animal: human is more specific, and their two opposites: not human and not animal; not human is more general than not animal, because not human applies to everything that not animal applies to, from everything disparate from animal, and its increase over it by applying to other than human from the types of animal. That is clear. And Ibn Marzuq indicated the four relationships between opposites by his saying: 'So the one of equality and absolute generality: the opposite of each of them is like it, so verify.' (1) On this there is an addition from the Amiriya edition only.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refinement responding to two objections about the classification of words according to their meanings. The first objection claims the division is not exhaustive because it omits contradiction (tanāquḍ). The commentator answers that contradiction falls under the first division, complete difference (tabāyun kullī), since contradictories like 'animal' and 'not animal' never coexist. Similarly, opposites like black and white (taḍādd) and correlatives like father and son (taḍāyuf) are also examples of complete difference. The second objection says that the second division's condition 'they do not differ at all' would include synonyms like 'insān' and 'bashar'. The commentator responds that the division concerns two different concepts (ma‘qūlayn); synonyms share a single concept, so they are excluded. Then the passage explains the relationships between the opposites of terms that have generality-particularity from one aspect (umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), giving detailed examples. For instance, 'human' and 'white' have generality from one aspect; their opposites 'not human' and 'not white' also have that relation. But if the two terms are 'animal' and 'not human', their opposites 'not animal' and 'human' are simply different. Finally, for terms with absolute generality-particularity (umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq), the relationship reverses: the opposite of the more general is more specific, and vice versa. The passage cites a poetic summary by Ibn Marzūq of the four possible relations between opposites.

A) Because contradiction is a separate, unlisted category.
B) Because contradiction is included in complete difference.
C) Because contradiction is the same as synonymy.
D) Because contradiction is not considered a valid relation.

Key Takeaway
The completeness of a classification depends on correctly identifying which categories subsume the apparent exceptions.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Determining the Relation Between Opposites of Terms with Generality and Particularity
Author explains how the relation between terms determines the relation between their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relationship between the opposites of two terms given their original relationship: generality-particularity from one aspect or absolute generality-particularity.

Core Idea
The relation between opposites mirrors the original except for absolute generality, where it reverses.

The author extends his analysis to the opposites of terms that are related by generality and particularity. He shows that the relationship between their negations can be deduced from the original relation. This lesson works through the three cases: from one aspect (two subcases) and absolute generality.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three original relationships
Terms can have generality-particularity from one aspect, absolute generality, or be completely different. The passage focuses on the first two.
We already covered complete difference in the previous lesson.
2
Apply the rule for from-one-aspect
If two terms have this relation (e.g., human and white), their opposites (not human, not white) also have the same relation: they combine in some things (crow), and each excludes something the other includes.
This is shown by the example of human and white.
3
Study the special case of from-one-aspect
When one term is the negation of a more general term (e.g., animal and not human), their opposites (not animal and human) become completely different (mutabāyinān).
This is because 'not animal' and 'human' have no overlap.
4
Apply the rule for absolute generality
For terms like animal (general) and human (specific), the opposites reverse: the opposite of the more general (not animal) is more specific than the opposite of the specific (not human).
Not human applies to more things than not animal.

(۱) على هذا زيادة من الطبعة الأميرية فقط .
Chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. If it is said: 'The division is not exhaustive because contradiction remains.' We say: 'It is included in the first division, which is complete difference, because two contradictories never come together at all, e.g., animal and not animal. And similarly, opposition like whiteness and blackness, and correlation like fatherhood and sonhood.' If you say: 'Your statement in the second division: that they do not differ at all includes synonyms, like human and man, and it is not valid upon this to count synonymy from the divisions of difference.' I say: 'Making the subject of the division the two concepts, i.e., the two different meanings, prevents the inclusion of synonyms due to the unity of their concept.' As for those two which have generality and particularity from one aspect, likewise their two opposites never have between them except generality and particularity from one aspect or difference. First: e.g., human and white; between them is generality and particularity from one aspect, and that is apparent. Their two opposites: (not human and not white) are likewise, because they combine in e.g., a crow, and the first is unique in snow, and the second in a black person. Second: e.g., animal and not human; between them is generality and particularity from one aspect, because they combine in e.g., a horse, and animal is unique in human, and not human in inorganic things; and their two opposites: not animal and human are necessarily different. As for those two which have absolute generality and particularity, it follows that between their two opposites there is absolute generality and particularity likewise, but reversed; i.e., the opposite of the more general is more specific, and the opposite of the more specific is more general. That is like human and animal: human is more specific, and their two opposites: not human and not animal; not human is more general than not animal, because not human applies to everything that not animal applies to, from everything disparate from animal, and its increase over it by applying to other than human from the types of animal. That is clear. And Ibn Marzuq indicated the four relationships between opposites by his saying: 'So the one of equality and absolute generality: the opposite of each of them is like it, so verify.' (1) On this there is an addition from the Amiriya edition only.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refinement responding to two objections about the classification of words according to their meanings. The first objection claims the division is not exhaustive because it omits contradiction (tanāquḍ). The commentator answers that contradiction falls under the first division, complete difference (tabāyun kullī), since contradictories like 'animal' and 'not animal' never coexist. Similarly, opposites like black and white (taḍādd) and correlatives like father and son (taḍāyuf) are also examples of complete difference. The second objection says that the second division's condition 'they do not differ at all' would include synonyms like 'insān' and 'bashar'. The commentator responds that the division concerns two different concepts (ma‘qūlayn); synonyms share a single concept, so they are excluded. Then the passage explains the relationships between the opposites of terms that have generality-particularity from one aspect (umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), giving detailed examples. For instance, 'human' and 'white' have generality from one aspect; their opposites 'not human' and 'not white' also have that relation. But if the two terms are 'animal' and 'not human', their opposites 'not animal' and 'human' are simply different. Finally, for terms with absolute generality-particularity (umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq), the relationship reverses: the opposite of the more general is more specific, and vice versa. The passage cites a poetic summary by Ibn Marzūq of the four possible relations between opposites.

What Is Mantiq?

Core Definition of Mushtarak
The author defines the mushtarak as a word with multiple meanings placed independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a word as mushtarak by checking whether its multiple meanings each have an independent original placement.

Core Idea
A mushtarak is a single word with multiple independently placed meanings.

In the fourfold classification of words, the third division is the mushtarak (homonym). The author defines it as a word that has multiple named entities (musammayāt), each established by a separate original placement (waḍ‘ ḥaqīqī). This definition distinguishes it from words that are merely ambiguous or metaphorical. Let's break down the definition and apply it to examples from the Arabic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines the mushtarak. Locate the formal definition: 'the word is one and its meaning is multiple' and the term mushtarak itself.
2
Extract the defining conditions
The definition has two key conditions: (1) the word is one, (2) its named entity (musammā) is multiple. Additionally, the placement must be real (waḍ‘ ḥaqīqī) and independent for each meaning, not derived from a transfer.
The commentator corrects 'meaning' to 'named entity' to exclude metaphor.
3
Test with examples
Apply the definition to ‘ayn: it has multiple named entities (eye, spring, coin) each placed independently. Similarly, qur’u has two meanings (purity and menstruation). Verify that there is no common core meaning.
The examples show mushtarak words are common nouns, not proper names.
4
Exclude non-examples
Why is insan not mushtarak? Because its named entity is one (the human species). This is a mufrad (univocal) word. The passage states that insan is mufrad.
Mufrad is the opposite of mushtarak.
5
Check the refinement on metaphor
The commentator notes that metaphor (majaz) is not mushtarak even though it has multiple meanings, because the second meaning is a transfer, not an independent placement. Thus, only words with multiple original placements qualify.
This refinement protects the technical definition.

القِسْمُ الثَّالِثُ مِنَ الأَقْسَامِ الأَرْبَعَةِ عِنْدَ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ، وَهُوَ : المُشْتَرَكُ - بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ -، وَهُوَ : مَا تَعَدَّدَ مُسَمَّاهُ؛
